The Mu'tazilite View: Will is Created and Subsisting in Itself
Statement of the Mu'tazilite position that God's will is created and subsisting in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that God's will is created and subsisting in itself, not an attribute of God's essence.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila held that will is a created entity subsisting in itself, not in God.

After presenting the Ash'ari proof, the author notes the Mu'tazilite alternative. They argue that will is a created accident (haditha) that exists in itself (qā'ima bi-dhātihā) and is not an attribute residing in God's essence. This lesson examines that claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite statement
Read the Arabic sentence describing the Mu'tazilite view. Note the key phrase: 'qā'ima bi-dhātihā' (subsisting in itself).
They attribute the view to specific figures: al-Jubba'i, 'Abd al-Jabbar.
2
Contrast with Ash'ari view
For the Ash'aris, will is an eternal attribute of God's essence. The Mu'tazila see it as a created thing, not an attribute.
3
Reflect on the implications
If will is created and not in God, then God's will is not eternal, which seems to contradict His perfection. The Ash'ari proof from the previous lesson challenges this.
This sets up the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : إرادته تعالى قديمة إذ لو كانت حادثة ولا شك أنها مستندة إلى المختار الذي هو ذاته تعالى لاحتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى مستندة إلى إرادة ثالثة وهكذا ولزم التسلسل في الإرادات الموجودة وقالت المعتزلة أي الجبائيات وعبد الجبار ومن تابعهم من المعتزلة : إنها حادثة قائمة بذاتها لا بذاته تعالى.
The second investigation: His will, exalted, is eternal, for if it were created, and there is no doubt that it is dependent upon the Chooser (al-mukhtār) who is His essence, it would need another will dependent upon a third will, and so on, and infinite regress would follow among existing wills. And the Mu'tazila — i.e., the followers of al-Jubba'i, 'Abd al-Jabbar, and those who followed them from the Mu'tazila — said that it is created, subsisting in itself, not in His essence.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage introduces the second investigation (al-baḥth al-thānī) concerning the attribute of will. The author presents the Ash'ari proof that God's will is eternal (qadīma): if it were created (ḥāditha), it would depend on the Chooser (God's essence), but as a contingent event it would require another will to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) of wills. This is impossible. The Mu'tazilite position is then stated: the will is created, subsisting in itself (qā'ima bi-dhātihā) and not in God's essence. The passage thus sets up the doctrinal divide on the nature of divine will.

Key Terms
حادثة
قائمة بذاتها
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Mu'tazila deny God's will altogether.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm that God has will, but they consider it a created entity, not an eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite view presented, God's will is:
A) Eternal and subsisting in God's essence.
B) Created and subsisting in itself.
C) Created and subsisting in God's essence.
D) Eternal and subsisting in itself.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view defines God's will as a created accident subsisting in itself, not an attribute of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever wrestled with the idea that God's will might change when your prayers are answered differently than you hoped? How does the belief that God's will is eternal and unchanging (unlike the Mu'tazilite view) affect your trust in His plan for your life? Write down one specific area where you need to submit to His will, even if it seems to change.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God's will must be created because otherwise it would imply temporal change in God. When you picture God, do you imagine Him as static or dynamic? Which view makes you feel closer to Him, and why? Spend a moment in gratitude that God's decisions are not reactions but part of an eternal, all-wise decree.

Active Knowledge as Specifier: Philosophical Objection and Ash'ari Response
Refutes philosophers' claim that active knowledge can specify, asserting necessity of equal relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' distinction between passive and active knowledge and the Ash'ari argument that even active knowledge cannot serve as a specifier due to the necessity of equal relation.

Core Idea
Active knowledge, like passive, has equal relation to opposites; only will can specify.

This passage presents a critical exchange where philosophers argue that God's active knowledge can specify which possibility occurs, unlike passive knowledge that follows what happens. The Ash'aris respond by appealing to a necessary truth: knowledge and power relate equally to both sides, so they cannot break the symmetry. Understanding this debate clarifies why Ash'aris insist on a distinct will in God.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the philosophical objection
Read the first part where philosophers distinguish passive knowledge (which follows the known) from active knowledge (which precedes and causes the known). They claim active knowledge can be a specifier.
Notice the key terms: العلم الانفعالي and العلم الفعلي.
2
Understand the Ash'ari reply
Read the Ash'ari response: they assert a self-evident truth that both knowledge and power have an equal relation to both sides of a possibility, so neither can be a specifier, even if it is active.
The phrase استواء النسبة إلى الطرفين is crucial.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument prevents active knowledge from specifying. Without a separate will, knowledge and power remain neutral. This shows the need for a distinct attribute of will in God.
Connect to broader Ash'ari theology on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما قال الحكماء: لا نسلم أن كل علم فهو تبع للوقوع وإنما ذلك في العلم الانفعالي التابع لوجود المعلوم، وأما العلم الفعلي الذي كلامنا فيه فإنه متبوع وسبب لوقوع المعلوم فيصلح أن يكون مخصصاً كما اخترناه في الباري سبحانه وتعالى. والأصحاب في جواب الحكماء يدعون الضرورة في استواء نسبة العلم والقدرة إلى الطرفين فلا يكون شيء منهما مخصصاً وإن كان العلم فعلياً.
And perhaps the philosophers said: We do not concede that every knowledge is consequent upon occurrence; that is only true of passive knowledge which follows the existence of the known. As for active knowledge, which is our concern, it is a source and cause for the occurrence of the known, so it can serve as a specifier, as we have chosen regarding the Exalted Creator. And the Companions [Ash'aris], in response to the philosophers, claim the necessity of the equal relation of knowledge and power to the two [opposite] sides, so neither of them can be a specifier, even if the knowledge is active.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers and the Ash'ari response concerning the specification of God's will. The philosophers argue that not all knowledge is dependent on the object; passive knowledge follows the known, but active knowledge precedes and causes it. Therefore, active knowledge can specify which possibility occurs, and this applies to God's knowledge. The Ash'aris reply by asserting a self-evident truth: knowledge and power have an equal relation to both sides of a possibility (existence and non-existence). Since neither knowledge nor power tilts towards one side, they cannot serve as a specifier, even if the knowledge is active. The argument hinges on the necessity of equal proportion, which prevents any intrinsic specification.

Key Terms
العلم الانفعالي
العلم الفعلي
المخصص
استواء النسبة
الطرفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Active knowledge, because it causes the known, can specify which possibility occurs without a will.
Correction: Even active knowledge has an equal relation to both sides; thus, without a will, it cannot specify. The Ash'aris argue that knowledge, whether passive or active, cannot break the symmetry.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response in this passage, why can active knowledge not be a specifier?
A) Because all knowledge is passive and follows the known.
B) Because knowledge and power have an equal relation to both sides of a possibility.
C) Because God's knowledge is not active.
D) Because specification requires will, not knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response argues that even active knowledge cannot specify because knowledge and power have an equal relation to both possibilities; only a distinct will can break the symmetry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you knew the right thing to do but did not do it. Why did your knowledge not specify your action? What was missing? How does the Ash'ari argument about the need for will parallel that? Now, what will you do next time you face such a choice?
?
Have you ever assumed that if you just knew enough, you would automatically do the right thing? Where has that assumption let you down? What change in your attitude towards knowledge and will is needed starting today?
?
This lesson shows that even God's active knowledge requires will to specify. How does that refute the idea that God's knowledge alone determines everything? What does that mean for your understanding of your own responsibility to choose and act?

God's Will Is Eternal
Proves divine will's eternity by rejecting temporalness, refuting two dissenting schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the eternity of God's will and the refutation of the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya positions.

Core Idea
If God's will were temporal, it would require an infinite chain of wills, which is impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a concise argument for the eternity of divine will. He then addresses two dissenting schools, showing that their views lead to an infinite regress. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the Islamic theological position on God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: thesis, conditional proof, then enumeration of opposing views.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the conditional statement: if will were temporal, it would need another will, causing infinite regress. Understand why this is impossible.
The argument relies on the principle that temporal events need a specifier.
3
Examine the refutations
Review the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya views. Note how the author refutes both with the same infinite regress argument plus additional points.
The Mu'tazila view is linked to philosophical emanation; the Karramiyya view is refuted by impossibility of temporal attributes in God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني: إرادته تعالى قديمة إذ لو كانت حادثة ولا شك أنها مستندة إلى المختار الذي هو ذاته تعالى لاحتاجت إلى إرادة أخرى مستندة إلى إرادة ثالثة وهكذا، ولزم التسلسل في الإرادات الموجودة. وقالت المعتزلة أي الجبائيات وعبد الجبار ومن تابعهم من المعتزلة: إنها حادثة قائمة بذاتها لا بذاته تعالى. فكأنه مأخوذ من قول الحكماء: إنه عند وجود المستعد للفيض يحصل الفيض. وقالت الكرامية: إنها حادثة قائمة بذاته تعالى. ويعرف بطلانهما بما ذكرنا من لزوم التسلسل في الإرادات، على أن قيام الصفة بذاتها غير معقول وقيام الحادث بذاته تعالى بطلانه.
The second investigation: His will, exalted is He, is eternal. For if it were temporal, and there is no doubt that it is dependent upon the Chooser who is His essence, it would need another will, dependent upon a third will, and so on, leading to an infinite regress in existing wills. And the Mu'tazila—namely the followers of al-Jubba'i, 'Abd al-Jabbar, and those who followed them among the Mu'tazila—said: It is temporal, subsisting by itself, not in His essence. It is as if it is taken from the saying of the philosophers: Upon the existence of that which is prepared for emanation, emanation occurs. And the Karramiyya said: It is temporal, subsisting in His essence. And the falsehood of both is known by what we mentioned: the necessity of infinite regress in wills, and because subsistence of an attribute by itself is unintelligible, and subsistence of a temporal entity in His essence is impossible.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the eternity of God's will (irada) by arguing from its contingency. The argument: if God's will were temporal (haditha), it would depend on a chooser (mukhtar) which is God's essence. But then that temporal will would itself require another will to specify its occurrence, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) in wills. This is impossible. Therefore, God's will is eternal (qadima). The passage then describes two opposing views: (1) The Mu'tazila claim God's will is temporal and subsists by itself (qa'im bi dhatiha), not in God's essence—an idea traced to the philosophers' notion of emanation. (2) The Karramiyya say it is temporal but subsists in God's essence. The author refutes both by noting that they lead to the same infinite regress, and additionally, an attribute subsisting by itself is impossible (since attributes require a subject), and a temporal entity subsisting in God's essence is also impossible because God's essence cannot be a locus for temporal accidents.

Key Terms
قديمة
حادثة
التسلسل
المختار
قائمة بذاتها
قيام الحادث بذاته تعالى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal will could be caused by God directly without another will.
Correction: The argument shows that a temporal will needs a specifier, which would have to be another will to avoid determinism, leading to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that God's will cannot be temporal?
A) Because temporal wills cannot cause anything.
B) Because a temporal will would require another will, causing infinite regress.
C) Because God's essence cannot have any attributes.
D) Because temporal things cannot be attributed to God.

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal because if it were temporal, an infinite regress of wills would follow, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that if God's will were temporal, an infinite chain of wills would be required. Reflect on a recent decision you made that you later wished God's will had been different. How does knowing that His will is eternal and unchanging challenge your trust in His plan? Are you willing to submit to His eternal will even when it contradicts your fleeting desires?
?
Both the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya held seemingly plausible views that led to logical contradictions. Can you identify an assumption you hold about God's nature that, upon honest examination, might lead to an infinite regress or absurdity? What would it mean for you to accept the simple truth that God's attributes are eternal and perfect, without your own mental modifications?
?
If God's will is eternal and not subject to change, how does that affect your daily prayers? Do you pray as if you are informing God of something new, or do you pray with the conviction that you are aligning yourself with His eternal decree? What would change in your prayer life if you truly believed that God's will was settled before time began?

Refutation of Deviant Views on Divine Will
Refutation of Mu'tazilite and Karramiyya views on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is a created accident subsisting in itself and the Karramiyya claim that it is a temporal accident subsisting in God's essence.

Core Idea
God's will cannot be an accident; neither subsisting in itself nor in God is possible.

After establishing that God possesses will as an eternal attribute, the author turns to refute two opposing views: that of the Mu'tazilites, who claimed will is a created accident subsisting in itself, and that of the Karramiyya, who claimed it is a temporal accident subsisting in God's essence. Both views are shown to be internally inconsistent and lead to absurd consequences, such as the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in itself or a temporal accident in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author first explains the Mu'tazilite position and its refutation, then the Karramiyya position.
2
Identify the two false views
The passage refutes two distinct positions. Locate where each is stated and note the specific claim made by each sect.
The Mu'tazilites are refuted first, then the Karramiyya.
3
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazilite view
The author gives a multi-part refutation: first, an attribute cannot subsist in itself; second, their interpretation of will as 'preparations' leads to belief in eternal matter and infinite regress; third, their real motivation was an erroneous understanding of the need for a temporal determiner.
The phrase 'لزوم التسلسل' (necessity of regress) is key.
4
Understand the refutation of the Karramiyya view
The Karramiyya view is dismissed briefly with two arguments: it leads to regress of wills, and a temporal accident cannot subsist in God's essence.
Notice the author uses 'بطلانهما' (their falsity) to group both refutations.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
Reflect on why these refutations matter: they protect the doctrine of God's eternal and perfect attributes, keeping divine will distinct from temporal events.
This connects to the broader discussion of divine attributes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأخذ على ما نقل عن المصنف أن قيام الصفة بذاتها يستلزم أن لا تكون صفة وهو ضروري البطلان فكأنهم أرادوا بالإرادة المعدات المكتنفة بالممكن الذي يحدث المادة وذلك لأن المعد يخصص وقوع المقدور على صفة معينة بوقت معين يتم فيه الاستعداد المستفاد منه ولا معنى للإرادة إلا الأمر المخصص كذلك والمعدات قائمة بذواتها فالإرادة بهذا المعنى قائمة بذاتها، وفيه بعد لأنه خروج عن قانون الملة إلى القول بوجود المادة القديمة واختصاص الحوادث بأوقاتها على حسب استعداداتها المتعاقبة إلى غير النهاية، والأظهر أن يقال : وجه الأخذ أنهم لما سمعوا مقالتهم هذه فهموا أن مخصص الحادث بوقته يجب أن يكون حادثاً فيه إذ لو كان موجوداً قبله لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح ولما اعتقدوا أن مخصص الحوادث إرادته تعالى حكموا بحدوثها، ولما لم يجوزوا قيام الحادث بذاته تعالى التجؤوا إلى أنها قائمة بذاتها . ( وقالت الكرامية : إنها حادثة قائمة بذاته تعالى، ويعرف بطلانهما بما ذكرنا من لزوم التسلسل في الإرادت، على أن قيام الصفة بذاتها غير معقول وقيام الحادث بذاته تعالى بطلانه .
The objection based on what is reported from the author: that the subsistence of an attribute in itself entails that it is not an attribute, and this is necessarily false. So it is as if they intended by 'will' the preparations surrounding the possible which generates matter, because the preparation specifies the occurrence of the predetermined thing in a specific manner at a specific time in which the disposition derived from it is completed, and there is no meaning of will except the specifying thing likewise, and the preparations subsist in themselves, so will in this sense subsists in itself. But this is far-fetched because it is a departure from the law of the religion to the doctrine of the existence of eternal matter and the specification of temporal events to their times according to their successive dispositions ad infinitum. The more correct view is to say: The point of the objection is that when they heard this statement of theirs, they understood that the determiner of a temporal event to its time must be temporal within it, for if it existed before it, there would be preponderance without a preponderator. And since they believed that the determiner of events is His will, they judged it to be temporal. And since they did not allow a temporal accident to subsist in God's essence, they resorted to it subsisting in itself. (And the Karramiyya said: It is temporal subsisting in God's essence. The falsity of both is known by what we have mentioned of the necessity of regress of wills, in addition to the fact that the subsistence of an attribute in itself is inconceivable, and the subsistence of a temporal accident in God's essence is false.)
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and explanation. The author begins by stating the objection reported from the author of al-Mawaqif: that an attribute subsisting in itself is impossible because it would cease to be an attribute. He then explains that the Mu'tazilites, to avoid this, interpreted 'will' as the 'preparations' (al-mu'iddat) which are accidents. Since preparations subsist in bodies, they claimed that will, understood as the specifying factor, also subsists in itself. The author rejects this interpretation because it violates Islamic law (qanun al-milla) by implying the existence of eternal matter and an infinite regress of dispositions. He then gives the preferred explanation: the Mu'tazilites, upon hearing that the determiner of a temporal event must itself be temporal, concluded that God's will is temporal. Since they deny that a temporal accident can subsist in God, they claimed it subsists in itself. This is refuted by the impossibility of an accident subsisting in itself and the regress of wills. Finally, the Karramiyya view that will is a temporal accident subsisting in God's essence is also refuted by the same arguments.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المعدات
الصفة
التخصيص
التسلسل
الحادث
القائم بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view that God's will is a created accident subsisting in itself is plausible because it avoids attributing a temporal accident to God.
Correction: The refutation shows this view leads to greater absurdities: it implies eternal matter and infinite regress, and an attribute cannot subsist in itself by definition.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence that the author argues follows from the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is a created accident subsisting in itself?
A) It implies God is not omnipotent.
B) It leads to the belief in eternal matter and an infinite regress of dispositions.
C) It makes will a mere metaphor.
D) It contradicts the unity of God.

Key Takeaway
Both the Mu'tazilite and Karramiyya views on divine will are false because they lead to logical absurdities (regress, impossibility of attribute in itself) and violate the principles of kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty in life, do you truly believe that God's will is eternal and all-encompassing, or do you subconsciously imagine it as a reaction to your actions? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in His immutable decree?
?
The Mu'tazilites were so concerned with protecting God's transcendence that they fell into greater absurdities. Are there areas in your faith where you rely too heavily on human logic rather than submitting to revelation? Will you commit to humbly accepting divine truths even when they seem paradoxical?
?
This lesson shows that deviant views of God's will lead to logical collapse. But do you live as if God's will is truly the ultimate reality? When you make plans, do you say 'insha'Allah' with genuine acknowledgment of His will, or is it a mere habit? What one change can you make today to align your heart more fully with the belief that God's will governs all?

Six Positions on God's Will: Al-Razi's Classification
al-Razi enumerates six positions on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six logical positions on the nature of God's will as enumerated by Imam al-Razi, and see how each branch unfolds from the initial dichotomy.

Core Idea
God's will is either His essence or not; every positive attribute must have a cause.

Imam al-Razi's classification of positions on God's will is a masterpiece of systematic theology. It begins with a simple dichotomy and then follows the logical consequences down to six distinct views. As you read, notice how the classification itself reveals which positions are coherent and which lead to problems.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial dichotomy
Read the first part where al-Razi splits positions into two: will identical to essence vs. not identical. Note the first view (Dirar).
2
Follow the negative vs. positive branch
The second branch splits further: either a negative attribute (al-Najjar) or a positive attribute. Understand what 'salbi' means here.
3
Trace the positive branch's causal chains
Positive attributes need a cause. Follow the subdivisions: caused by essence, or by something else that is either beginningless or temporal, etc. Count the total positions as you go.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة : في ضبط مذاهب المتكلمين في كونه تعالى مريداً ( قال الإمام الرازي) في الأربعين : ( كونه تعالى مريداً إما أن يكون نفس ذاته وهو قول ضرار وإما أن لا يكون نفس ذاته (و) حينئذ (إما ) أن يكون أمراً سلبياً وهو قول النجار) كما مر من كونه غير مغلوب ولا مكره ( وإما ) (ثبوتياً) ولا بد له من علة لإمكانه، فيكون (إما معللاً بذاته ) تعالى ( وهو القول الآخر له وإما معللاً) بغير ذاته، وحينئذ إما أن يعلل ) بمعنى قديم ) قائم بذاته تعالى ( وهو قول أصحابنا، وإما حادث إما قائم بذاته تعالى وهو قول الكرامية أو موجود لا في محل وهو قول الجبائية ) وعبد الجبار ( من المعتزلة أو قائم بذات غير ذات الله تعالى، ولم نر أحداً ذهب إليه .
Conclusion: On cataloging the schools of the theologians concerning His being willing. (Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba'in:) His being willing is either identical to His essence—this is the view of Dirar—or not identical to His essence. In that case, it is either a negation (salbi)—this is the view of al-Najjar, as previously mentioned concerning His being neither overpowered nor compelled—or a positive attribute (thubuti). It must have a cause for its possibility. Thus it is either caused by His essence—this is the other view of al-Najjar—or caused by something else. In that case, the cause is either a beginningless (qadim) accident subsisting in His essence—this is the view of our fellows (Ash'aris)—or a temporal accident, either subsisting in His essence—this is the view of the Karramiyya—or existing not in a locus (mahall)—this is the view of al-Jubba'i and 'Abd al-Jabbar from the Mu'tazila—or subsisting in an essence other than God's essence, and we have not seen anyone who held this view.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a classification (ضبط) of the different theological schools regarding God's will. The author first introduces it as a 'conclusion' (خاتمة) and then quotes Imam al-Razi from his work al-Arba'in. Al-Razi divides the positions into two main branches: either God's will is identical to His essence (the view of Dirar) or not. If not, it is either a negative attribute (al-Najjar's first view) or a positive attribute. The positive attribute must have a cause. That cause is either God's essence (al-Najjar's second view) or something else. If something else, the cause is either: (a) a beginningless accident subsisting in God (the Ash'ari view), (b) a temporal accident subsisting in God (Karramiyya), (c) a temporal accident not in a locus (the Mu'tazilite view of al-Jubba'i and 'Abd al-Jabbar), or (d) a temporal accident subsisting in another essence (no one held this). Thus six positions are enumerated. The following discussion (not in this lesson) will invalidate all except the Ash'ari position.

Key Terms
نفس الذات
أمر سلبي
أمر ثبوتي
معلل
قديم
حادث
لا في محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All six positions are equally valid options.
Correction: Al-Razi's classification is a logical derivation showing how each position entails contradictions; only the Ash'ari position (beginningless accident in essence) is considered coherent.

Checkpoint
How many distinct positions does al-Razi list regarding the nature of God's will?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi systematically derives six logical positions on God's will, eliminating all but the Ash'ari view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God wills' in your daily prayers, do you imagine His will as something that changes with time? How does the logical necessity of a beginningless cause challenge that assumption?
?
If the only coherent position is that God's will is a beginningless accident subsisting in His essence, what does that mean for how you understand your own will—which is temporal and dependent? Does this realization humble you?
?
This lesson shows that theologians spent great effort to avoid attributing temporality to God. Is your own concept of God careful enough to avoid such mistakes, or do you sometimes think of God in human terms without realizing it?

Al-Razi's Classification of Six Positions on Divine Will
Al-Razi enumerates the six positions on divine will via logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical tree al-Razi uses to classify the six positions on whether God is willing.

Core Idea
Divine will is classified along a six-branch logical tree.

Al-Razi begins by laying out the possible positions on divine will. Starting from whether it is identical to the essence or not, he branches into negative and positive, and then into causes. This classification sets the stage for theological evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the root dichotomy
Al-Razi first divides positions into those that equate God's willing with His essence and those that do not. Notice the two main branches.
Dirar holds the first; others hold the second.
2
Follow the negative branch
For those who say it is not the essence, one sub-branch is a negative attribute (al-Najjar's first view). See the example: not being overcome or coerced.
3
Explore the positive branch
The positive branch further divides into whether the will is caused by the essence or by something else. This leads to the Ash'ari, Karami, and Mu'tazili views.
Each has distinct implications.
4
Identify the seventh unattested view
Finally, al-Razi mentions a seventh possibility (subsisting in another essence) but notes no one holds it.
This shows completeness of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : في أنه تعالى مريد
وهو أحد الإمام الرازي) في الأربعين : ( كونه تعالى مريداً إما أن يكون نفس ذاته وهو قول ضرار وإما أن لا يكون نفس ذاته (و) حينئذ (إما ) أن يكون أمراً سلبياً قولي النجار) كما مر من كونه غير مغلوب ولا مكره ( وإما ) (ثبوتياً) ولا بد له من علة لإمكانه، فيكون (إما معللاً بذاته ) تعالى ( وهو القول الآخر له وإما معللاً) بغير ذاته، وحينئذ إما أن يعلل ) بمعنى قديم ) قائم بذاته تعالى ( وهو قول أصحابنا، وإما حادث إما قائم بذاته تعالى وهو قول الكرامية أو موجود لا في محل وهو قول الجبائية ) وعبد الجبار ( من المعتزلة أو قائم بذات غير ذات الله تعالى، ولم نر أحداً ذهب إليه . ويبطل الأول أنا نعلمه ونشك في كونه مريداً و) يبطل (الثاني لزوم كون الجماد مريداً ) لأنه غير مغلوب (و) يبطل ( الخامس والسادس لزوم التسلسل) في الإرادات (و) يبطل ( الخامس خاصة أنه لا يقوم الحادث بذاته تعالى والسادس) خاصة أنه يلزم عرض لا في محل وأن نسبة ما لا محل له إلى جميع الذوات سواء فإذا مثلاً ماذا، ثم إنه ذكر فيه مذاهب ستة وقد أبطل الأولين والآخرين، والرابع هو المذهب الحق فلذا لم يقدح فيه وأما الثالث فوجه عدم التعرض لإبطاله لا يخلو عن خفاء ولعل وجه أن كونه تعالى مريداً هو المريدية وهي نفس إرادة عند ضرار القائل : بأنه عين الذات ، ولهذا ذكر في بعض كتب الكلام أن ضراراً يقول : بأن الإرادة عين الذات، وهكذا عند النجار في أحد قوليه وهو أنه أمر ثبوتي معلل بذاته تعالى فهو في التحقيق موافق لمذهب أهل السنة ولذا لم يتعرض لإبطاله وإنما أورد مذهب الأصحاب مقابلا له باعتبار أن المريدية عندهم ليست عين الإرادة والأمر في ذلك بعد القول بالإرادة وتعللها بالذات هين.
The Fourth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: On that He, exalted, is Willing. And it is one of Imam al-Razi in al-Arba'in: (His being, exalted, willing is either being His essence itself, and this is the saying of Dirar, or not being His essence itself, and then either being a negative thing, the saying of al-Najjar, as previously mentioned of His being not overcome nor coerced, or a positive thing, and it necessarily has a cause for its contingency, so it is either caused by His essence, exalted, and this is the other saying of his, or caused by other than His essence, and then either caused by an eternal meaning subsisting in His essence, exalted, and this is the saying of our companions, or an accident either subsisting in His essence, exalted, and this is the saying of the Karamiyya, or existing in no locus, and this is the saying of al-Jubba'i and 'Abd al-Jabbar from the Mu'tazila, or subsisting in an essence other than the essence of Allah, exalted, and we have not seen anyone go to it. And the first is invalidated by that we know Him and doubt His being willing; and the second is invalidated by the necessity of lifeless objects being willing because He is not overcome; and the fifth and sixth are invalidated by the necessity of regress in wills; and the fifth specifically is invalidated by that the accident does not subsist in His essence, exalted; and the sixth specifically is invalidated by that it necessitates an accident in no locus and that the relation of that which has no locus to all essences is equal, so for example, what then? Then he mentioned in it six schools, and he invalidated the first two and the last two, and the fourth is the true school, so he did not find fault with it. As for the third, the reason for not addressing its invalidation is not free from obscurity, and perhaps the reason is that His being, exalted, willing is the willfulness, and it is the will itself according to Dirar who says that it is the essence itself, and for this it is mentioned in some books of kalam that Dirar says that the will is the essence itself, and likewise according to al-Najjar in one of his two sayings, and it is that it is a positive thing caused by His essence, exalted, so in reality it agrees with the school of Ahl al-Sunna, and therefore he did not address its invalidation, and he only presented the school of the companions as opposed to it considering that willfulness according to them is not the will itself, and the matter in that after affirming the will and its being caused by the essence is easy.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents a classification and evaluation of the six positions (madhaahib) regarding the divine attribute of will (iraadah). The author, Imam al-Razi, enumerates the views in a logical tree structure. The first position (Dirar's) equates God's being willing with His essence. The second (al-Najjar's) makes it a negative attribute (not being overcome or coerced). The remaining four are positive: (3) caused by the essence (another view of al-Najjar), (4) caused by an eternal meaning subsisting in the essence (the Ash'ari view, 'our companions'), (5) a created accident subsisting in the essence (Karamiyya), and (6) a created accident in no locus (al-Jubba'i and 'Abd al-Jabbar). A seventh (subsisting in another essence) is dismissed as unattested. Al-Razi then refutes positions 1, 2, 5, and 6, leaving only positions 3 and 4 as possibilities. He explains why position 3 (al-Najjar's second view) is not explicitly refuted: it effectively agrees with Ahl al-Sunna in making will a positive attribute caused by the essence, differing only in terminology.

C) The Ash'ari view (our companions)

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi classifies six positions on divine will using a logical tree, with the root being identity or non-identity with the essence.

Al-Razi's Evaluation of the Six Positions on Divine Will
Al-Razi refutes four of the six positions and explains the status of the remaining two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi refutes positions 1, 2, 5, and 6, accepts position 4, and why position 3 is not explicitly refuted.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's refutations narrow the valid views on divine will to the Ash'ari position.

After enumerating the positions, al-Razi applies logical refutations. He invalidates the extremes and preserves the Ash'ari view as the sound one. He also explains why al-Najjar's second view is not explicitly refuted, as it effectively aligns with Sunni doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify which positions are refuted
Al-Razi refutes positions 1 (Dirar), 2 (al-Najjar's negative), 5 (Karamiyya), and 6 (Mu'tazila). Note which are left standing.
Positions 3 and 4 are not refuted.
2
Examine the refutation of the first two
The first is refuted because we know God exists but may doubt His will. The second is refuted because it would imply that lifeless objects, which are not overcome or coerced, would also be willing.
3
Examine the refutation of the last two
The fifth and sixth are refuted because they lead to infinite regress of wills. Additionally, the fifth fails because accidents cannot subsist in God; the sixth fails because an accident in no locus is incoherent.
4
Understand why the third is not refuted
Al-Razi's third position (positive, caused by essence) is effectively the same as the fourth in meaning, just phrased differently. Dirar and al-Najjar's second view say God's willing is the will itself (identical to essence or its property), which aligns with Ahl al-Sunna in affirming a positive attribute.
Thus, al-Razi only presents the Ash'ari view as the explicit true school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of the Negative Attribute View of Will
Objection and response regarding negative definition of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a negative definition of divine will leads to absurdities (like attributing will to lifeless objects) and the responses involving circularity and scope.

Core Idea
A negative definition of will cannot be restricted to God without circularity or absurdity.

This passage addresses a key objection against al-Najjar's view that divine will is merely the absence of being overcome or coerced. The critic argues that this definition would wrongly imply that lifeless objects have will, since they also are not overcome. The commentary examines possible defenses and their flaws, revealing deeper issues of definition and circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the initial objection: that defining will as 'not overcome' makes lifeless objects 'willing'.
The objection is straightforward: lifeless objects are also not overcome.
2
Examine the first defense and its counter
The first defense restricts the definition to voluntary actions. But this leads to circularity because voluntary actions are defined as willed.
Circularity (دور) is a key logical flaw.
3
Consider the second defense and remaining problem
Another defense treats it as a verbal definition. However, the negative attribute still applies to non-choosing beings, so the objection remains.
Verbal definitions do not solve logical scope issues.
4
Reflect on the final argument and its rebuttal
The commentary from Sharh al-Maqasid argues the definition is restricted by context, but the refuter insists the same justification applies to lifeless objects.
This debate shows the depth of the theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( ويبطل الثاني لزوم كون الجماد إلخ ) قد يجاب عنه بأن المراد كونه غير مغلوب ولا مكره في أفعاله الاختيارية فلا يلزم كون الجماد مريداً ، وفيه أن الأفعال الاختيارية هي الإرادية فيؤول إلى تفسير الإرادة بعدم المغلوبية والمكرهية في الأفعال الإرادية وأنه دور ظاهر. اللهم إلا أن يجعل ما ذكر تعريفاً لفظياً للإرادة على أن أصل الاعتراض باق لأن عدم المغلوبية في الفعل الاختياري أمر سلبي يصدق بعدم الاختيار فيتناول الجماد قال في شرح المقاصد : الاعتراض على قول النجار بأنه يوجب كون الجماد مريداً ليس بشيء لأنه إنما يفسر بذلك إرادة الله تعالى فإن قلت خصوص المحدود لا يفيد تخصيص الحد والألم لم يحتج إلى اشتراط المساواة . قلت : مراده أن في الحد قيدا يخصصه وهو ضمير كونه الراجع إليه تعالى لكن يرد عليه أن مقصود المبطل أنه لو صح إطلاق المريد عليه تعالى بمجرد ذلك لصح إطلاقه على الجماد لقيام مصحح الإطلاق فيه أيضاً فليتأمل.
His saying: (And the second is invalidated by the necessity of lifeless objects being willing, etc.) It may be answered that what is meant is His being not overcome nor coerced in His voluntary actions, so the necessity of lifeless objects being willing does not follow. But [it is objected] in it that voluntary actions are volitional, so it leads to interpreting will as not being overcome nor coerced in volitional actions, and that is an apparent circularity. However, if what is mentioned is taken as a verbal definition of will, then the original objection remains because not being overcome in voluntary action is a negative thing that is true of non-choice, so it includes lifeless objects. He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: The objection against al-Najjar's saying that it necessitates lifeless objects being willing is nothing, because he only interprets by that the will of God, exalted. If you say: the specificity of the defined does not benefit the specification of the definition, [then] he did not need to stipulate equality. I say: his meaning is that in the definition there is a restriction that specifies it, which is the pronoun of 'His being' referring back to Him, exalted. But it is objected to him that the intention of the refuter is that if the application of 'willing' to Him, exalted, is valid merely by that, then its application to lifeless objects would also be valid due to the existence of the justification for application in it as well. So reflect.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage addresses the refutation of al-Najjar's position that divine will is a negative attribute (not being overcome or coerced). The objection is that this would make lifeless objects 'willing' since they are also not overcome. The commentary presents a possible defense: the definition applies specifically to voluntary actions. But this leads to circularity because voluntary actions are defined as willed. Alternatively, if it is a verbal definition, the original objection persists because the negative attribute 'not overcome' can apply to non-choosing beings. The commentary then quotes from Sharh al-Maqasid, arguing that the definition is restricted to God by the pronoun ('His'), but the refuter counters that if the justification for applying 'willing' to God is the same negative attribute, it also justifies applying it to lifeless objects. The passage ends by calling for reflection.

Key Terms
مغلوب
مكره
أفعال اختيارية
دور
تعريف لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The negative definition of divine will is easily dismissed because it applies to lifeless objects.
Correction: The debate involves nuanced distinctions about definition scope, circularity, and whether the definition is real or verbal.

Checkpoint
What is the primary objection against al-Najjar's definition of divine will as 'not being overcome or coerced'?
A) It implies that God's will is not eternal
B) It would also apply to lifeless objects, making them 'willing'
C) It contradicts the concept of divine power
D) It is a positive attribute, not a negative one

Key Takeaway
Defining divine will as a negative attribute leads to logical problems, as it either applies too broadly or leads to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's will, do you sometimes reduce it to a mere absence of coercion in your mind? How does that diminish your sense of His active, personal involvement in your life? What will you do today to remind yourself that God's will is positive and purposeful, not just a lack of constraint?
?
The circularity in defining will by voluntary actions mirrors how we sometimes define faith by external obedience without examining the heart. Where in your life have you defined your relationship with God by what you avoid rather than what you actively pursue? What one concrete step will you take this week to move from a negative to a positive definition of your devotion?
?
The objection that lifeless objects could be called 'willing' under a negative definition shows the danger of reducing divine attributes to mere negations. In your own understanding of God, are there attributes you think of more in terms of what they deny than what they affirm? How can you begin to affirm God's will as an active, choosing will that reaches into your daily decisions?

Refuting the Accident in No Locus: Objection and Reply
Commentary on objections to the refutation of accident in no locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections to the refutation of the 'accident in no locus' position and the nuanced replies involving agency and conceptual attributes.

Core Idea
A negative attribute cannot be a positive cause—unless the effect is merely conceptual.

This passage continues the discussion of the sixth position (will as an accident in no locus). The commentator addresses an objection from Sharh al-Maqasid that challenges the refutation by pointing out that if will subsists by itself, God has no special right to it. The reply involves God's agency and the conceptual nature of specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three distinct comments. The first is an objection, the second a reply about agency, the third a reply about conceptual attributes.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection from Sharh al-Maqasid
The first comment quotes an objection: if will subsists by itself, God is not more entitled to it than another. Also, God being in no locus is negative and cannot be a cause for positive specification.
3
Examine the reply about agency
The second comment counters that God is the agent of the will, and an agent's relation to its own effect is not equal to another's. This replies to the equality objection.
Note the phrase 'وفيه ما فيه' indicating the reply is not fully decisive.
4
Consider the third comment on conceptual attributes
The third comment addresses the claim that a negative cannot be a cause for affirmation. It replies that if the effect is a conceptual description (not a real existent), then a negative attribute can be its source.
This shows the refutation's limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( أنه يلزم عرض لا في محل اعترض عليه في شرح المقاصد بأن صفات الباري كانت الإرادة قائمة بذاتها فليس كونه تعالى مريداً بها أولى من كون غيره مريداً بها ) وكون ذاته تعالى لا في (محل كتلك الإرادة ) لا يوجب اختصاصه به) لأن كونه لا في محل أمر سلبي فلا يكون علة للثبوت.
قوله: ( وإن نسبة ما لا محل له إلى جميع الذوات (سواء قيل : لا نسلم استواء النسبة فإن ذات الله تعالى فاعل للإرادة واختصاص الفاعل بالأثر الغير القائم بغيره أولى من اختصاص غيره به وفيه ما فيه .
قوله: ( فلا يكون علة للثبوت) قيل : هذا إنما يتم إذا كان المعلول أمراً موجوداً في الخارج أما إذا كان وصفاً اعتبارياً فيجوز أن يكون منشؤه وصفاً اعتبارياً أيضاً وهاهنا كذلك لأن كون الإرادة بحيث تختص بتلك الذات لا بغيرها من الذوات اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان .
His saying: (that it necessitates an accident in no locus. It was objected in Sharh al-Maqasid that the attributes of the Creator—if the will subsisted by itself—then His being, exalted, willing by it is not more appropriate than another being willing by it.) And His essence, exalted, being in no locus like that will does not necessitate His being specified by it, because His being in no locus is a negative thing, so it cannot be a cause for affirmation. His saying: (And the relation of that which has no locus to all essences is equal) It is said: We do not accept the equality of the relation, for the essence of Allah, exalted, is an agent of the will, and the agent's specification with the effect that does not subsist in another is more appropriate than another's specification with it, and in it is [room for reflection]. His saying: (so it cannot be a cause for affirmation) It is said: This is only complete if the effect is something existent in the external world. But if it is a conceptual description, it is permissible that its source be a conceptual description as well, and here it is so, because the will being such that it is specific to that essence and not to other essences is conceptual, having no reality in concrete beings.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage contains three comments on the refutation of the fifth and sixth positions. The first comment addresses the sixth position (will as an accident in no locus). An objection from Sharh al-Maqasid states that if will subsists by itself, then God is not more entitled to be called willing by it than any other being. Moreover, God's being in no locus (like the will) is a negative attribute and cannot be a cause (علة) for a positive determination (ثبوت) of specification. The second comment counters a possible objection that the relation of a no-locus accident to all essences is equal; it replies that God is the agent of the will, and an agent's relation to its own effect is not equal. The third comment examines the statement that a negative cannot be a cause for affirmation; it notes that if the effect (the specification of the will to God) is a conceptual (اعتباري) description, then a negative attribute can be its source, since the specification is not a concrete reality. Thus the refutation may not be absolute.

Key Terms
عرض لا في محل
سلبي
ثبوتي
اعتباري
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of 'accident in no locus' is decisive and cannot be challenged.
Correction: The commentary shows that the refutation faces objections, and its success depends on whether the specification is real or conceptual.

Checkpoint
According to the third comment, why might a negative attribute (like 'being in no locus') still serve as a cause for specification?
A) Because God's will is eternal
B) Because the specification is a conceptual description, not a concrete existent
C) Because negative attributes are always effective
D) Because the will is an accident

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the 'accident in no locus' position is not straightforward; objections based on agency and the conceptual nature of specification must be addressed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's will is 'conceptual' and not a physical property, does that make His sovereignty feel more abstract or more real to you? How does your heart truly respond to the idea that His attributes are beyond physical categories?
?
In your daily life, do you treat concepts like 'mercy' or 'justice' as merely mental ideas or as realities that demand action? How can the distinction between real and conceptual in this lesson challenge you to take your beliefs more seriously?
?
If God's negative attributes (like not being in a place) can still give rise to positive conceptual realities, what does that imply about how you should approach understanding His perfection? Are there areas where you have limited God to physical or worldly categories, missing the depth of His transcendence?

Divine Hearing and Sight: Known by Religious Necessity
Author asserts religious necessity of divine hearing and sight and defends against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attributes of hearing and sight are established by religious necessity from the Qur'an and hadith, requiring no rational proof.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight are religious certainties, not rational inferences.

The author begins the sixth objective by asserting that God's hearing and sight are known by necessity from the religion of Muhammad. This means every Muslim knows these attributes from the Qur'an and hadith without needing philosophical arguments. In this lesson, we explore why this is the case and how the author defends this claim against objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of knowledge
Notice that the author says hearing and sight are known 'by necessity from the religion of Muhammad'. This means the evidence is direct from revelation, not reason. Read the Arabic and translation to grasp this claim.
The phrase 'ضرورة دينية' is key.
2
Examine the evidence from Qur'an and hadith
The author points to the Qur'an and hadith being filled with references to God's hearing and sight. Consider how this makes denial or interpretation impossible.
The commentator clarifies that 'filled' refers to both scriptures individually.
3
Understand the objection about other attributes
The author answers why other attributes like knowledge and power are not claimed as religious necessities. Think about the logical reason: the Law's validity depends on those attributes.
This shows the author's careful distinction.
4
Address the Ash'ari interpretation
The author dismisses the Ash'ari interpretation of hearing and sight as knowledge of what is heard and seen. Note that it does not negate the literal application affirmed by scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : في أنه تعالى سميع بصير
أنه تعالى سميع بصير السمع دل عليه وهو مما علم بالضرورة من دير محمد ) فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال عليه كما هو حق سائر الضروريات الدينية حي ) والقرآن و ( كذا الحديث مملوء به بحيث لا يمكن إنكاره ولا تأويله ) لأنه معلوم ضروري بلا اشتباه فيه ( وقد احتج عليه بعض الأصحاب بأنه تعالى وكل حي اتصافه بالسمع والبصر ومن صح اتصافه بصفة اتصف بها أو بضدها وضد السمع والبصر هو الصمم والعمى وإنهما من صفات النقص فامتنع اتصافه تعالى بهما فوجب ) اتصافه (بالسمع ويتوقف هذا الاحتجاج على مقدمات) لا صحة لها، يصح تعالى ليست من قبيل الأعراض عندهم . والجواب أن هذا القول منهم على تقدير قدم الصفات، إذ العرضية تستلزم التجدد المنافي لذلك القدم وإلا فعلى تقدير حدوثها إنكار عرضيتها مما لا يلتفت إليه كما أشار إليه الشارح في أول مباحث الأعراض.
قوله: ( فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال (عليه فإن قلت لا فرق في ذلك بينهما وبين العالم والقادر والحي والمريد فلم لم يدع الضرورة الدينية في هذه وادعى فيهما قلت : ثبوت الشرع تتوقف على هذه الصفات فلا يمكن فيها دعوى الضرورة الدينية بخلاف السمع والبصر كما هو الظاهر. قوله: ( وكذا الحديث مملوء به أشار الشارح بإقحام لفظ كذا إلى أن ضمير مملوء راجع إلى كل من القرآن والحديث لا إلى المجموع ولذا لم يقل مملوءان . قوله: (ولا) تأويله أي لا يمكن تأويل كل من الكتاب والسنة بحيث لا يثبت فيه إطلاق السمع والبصر عليه تعالى فلا يقدح فيما ذكره تأويل الأشعري السمع والبصر بالعلم بالمسموع والمبصر .
قوله: ( على مقدمات لا صحة لها أي لجميعها بل الصحة إنما هي لبعضها، وهي الرابعة فتأمل .
The Sixth Objective: On that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing. That He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing. Hearing indicates it, and it is known by necessity from the religion of Muhammad, so there is no need for inference upon it, as is the right of all religious necessities. And the Qur'an and likewise the hadith are filled with it such that it cannot be denied nor interpreted, because it is a necessary known without any confusion. And some of the companions argued for it that He, exalted, is every living being, and the description with hearing and sight [applies to Him], and whoever's description with a quality is valid, he is described by it or by its opposite. And the opposite of hearing and sight is deafness and blindness, and they are attributes of deficiency, so His description, exalted, with them is impossible, so description with hearing and sight is necessary. And this argument depends on premises that are not sound. [The argument] that [the attributes of] the Exalted are not of the kind of accidents according to them. And the answer is that this statement of theirs is based on the assumption of the eternity of attributes, since accidentally necessitates renewal, which is incompatible with that eternity. Otherwise, on the assumption of their temporal origination, the denial of their accidentally is something not to be paid attention to, as the commentator indicated in the beginning of the discussion of accidents. His saying: (so there is no need for inference upon it). For if you say: there is no difference between them and the attributes of knowledge, power, life, and will, so why is religious necessity not claimed for these and claimed for those? I say: the establishment of the Law depends on these attributes, so religious necessity cannot be claimed for them, unlike hearing and sight, as is apparent. His saying: (And likewise the hadith is filled with it). The commentator indicated by inserting the word 'likewise' that the pronoun of 'filled' refers to each of the Qur'an and the hadith, not to the sum, and therefore he did not say 'filled (dual)'. His saying: (and not its interpretation), i.e., it is not possible to interpret each of the Book and the Sunna such that the application of hearing and sight to Him, exalted, is not established, so al-Ash'ari's interpretation of hearing and sight as knowledge of what is heard and what is seen does not harm what he mentioned. His saying: (on premises that are not sound), i.e., all of them, rather soundness is only for some of them, and it is the fourth, so reflect.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth objective: God is Hearing (Samii') and Seeing (Basiiir). It asserts that this is known by religious necessity (ضرورة دينية) from the Qur'an and hadith, so no rational proof is needed. However, some companions attempted a rational proof: since God is living, and every living being must have either hearing/sight or their opposites (deafness/blindness), and the opposites are deficiencies, therefore God must have hearing and sight. The author critiques this proof, noting that its premises are unsound; for instance, God's attributes are not accidents (أعراض) requiring such inference. The commentary then addresses several points: (1) Why is hearing/sight considered necessary knowledge while other attributes like knowledge and power are not? Because the Law's validity depends on those latter attributes, so they cannot be taken as merely necessary. (2) The phrase 'and likewise the hadith' indicates that the pronoun 'filled' refers separately to both Qur'an and hadith. (3) The possibility of interpreting hearing and sight as knowledge (the Ash'ari view) does not negate the literal application affirmed by scripture. (4) The premises of the proof are not all sound; only the fourth premise might be valid, but this is left for reflection.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
ضرورة دينية
تأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that hearing and sight require rational proof.
Correction: The author emphasizes they are religious necessities not requiring inference.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say no rational proof is needed for God's hearing and sight?
A) Because reason cannot prove them
B) Because they are known by religious necessity from Qur'an and hadith
C) Because they are self-evident to all humans
D) Because the proof would lead to anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are known by religious necessity from revelation, not rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God hears and sees every single word you utter and every action you do, or have you reduced these attributes to abstract concepts that have no real impact on your daily choices?
?
If you were asked to defend the belief that God hears and sees using only the Qur'an and hadith, could you do so without relying on rational arguments? What does that say about your reliance on revelation?
?
In your private moments, when you are alone and no human sees you, does the certainty that God sees and hears you change how you act? If not, what is preventing that belief from transforming your behavior?

Critique of the Rational Proof for God's Hearing and Sight
Author presents and critiques a rational proof for divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's hearing and sight and the author's critique of its premises.

Core Idea
Rational proofs for divine attributes can be unsound; religious necessity suffices.

Some companions attempted a rational proof for God's hearing and sight based on the necessity of a living being having either these attributes or their opposites. The author critiques this proof, arguing that its premises are unsound. In this lesson, we examine the proof and the critique, including the issue of attributes being accidents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational proof
Read the proof: Every living being must have either hearing/sight or their opposites (deafness/blindness). Since opposites are deficiencies, God must have hearing and sight.
Note the logic of alternative.
2
Identify the premises
The author says the proof depends on premises that are not sound. Try to list the premises and see which might be problematic.
The commentator mentions the fourth premise might be sound, so reflect.
3
Explore the critique about accidental attributes
The author critiques that God's attributes are not accidents. Understand why this matters: accidentally implies temporality, which contradicts the eternity of attributes.

Refutation of an Argument on God's Will
Refutes the claim that God's will being in His essence makes Him uniquely willing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's will being in His essence does not make Him uniquely willing, and why a negative attribute cannot establish a positive reality.

Core Idea
A negative attribute cannot be a cause for a positive reality.

In this passage, the author refutes an argument claiming that God's will must be uniquely His because His essence is not in a place. The refutation clarifies that a negative description cannot confer positive exclusivity. By studying this, you will see the logical flaw in using negative attributes to prove positive distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and two distinct refutation points.
2
Identify the two refutation points
The passage first argues that if the will subsists in God's essence, it could equally subsist in another, so no uniqueness. Then it argues that God's essence being 'not in a place' is negative and cannot cause a positive reality like exclusive possession.
The second point is crucial: negatives don't produce positives.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Why can't a negative attribute be a cause for affirmation? This principle applies widely in kalam arguments about divine attributes.
Consider how this refutes the opponent's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت الإرادة قائمة بذاتها فليس كونه تعالى مريداً بها أولى من كون غيره مريداً بها، وكون ذاته تعالى لا في محل كتلك الإرادة لا يوجب اختصاصه به، لأن كونه لا في محل أمر سلبي فلا يكون علة للثبوت.
If the will were subsisting in His essence, then His being willing by it would not be more appropriate than another being willing by it. And His essence not being in a place like that will does not necessitate His exclusivity by it, because His not being in a place is a negative matter, so it cannot be a cause for affirmation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument concerning the attribute of will (irada). The argument claimed that if God's will is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, then He is uniquely characterized by it because His essence is not in a place. The refutation proceeds in two steps. First, if the will subsists in God's essence, then we could say the same about any other essence: if another essence had that will subsisting in it, that other would also be willing by it. So God's being willing by it is not more appropriate than another's. Second, the claim that because God's essence is not in a place (la fi mahall) while the will is in a place (i.e., subsisting in an essence) and this makes Him exclusive is rejected because 'not being in a place' is a negative attribute. A negative attribute cannot be a cause for a positive reality (thubut). Therefore, the exclusivity is not proven.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
محل (mahall)
سلبي (salbi)
علة للثبوت (illa li'l-thubut)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a negative attribute like 'not in a place' can confer positive uniqueness.
Correction: As the passage states, a negative attribute cannot be a cause for affirmation; it lacks the power to establish a positive reality.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does God's essence being 'not in a place' not establish His exclusivity in having the attribute of will?
A) Because 'not in a place' is a negative attribute and cannot be a cause for affirmation.
B) Because God's will is not really in His essence.
C) Because other beings also have wills.
D) Because the will is not an attribute.

Key Takeaway
A negative attribute cannot be a cause for a positive reality, so using 'not in a place' to prove exclusive will fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defined yourself by what you are not (e.g., 'not a sinner', 'not ignorant') and assumed that gives you a positive status before God? This lesson shows that a negative cannot produce a positive—where might you be relying on negatives to feel spiritually secure? What would it take to instead seek a positive reality?
?
Consider an area where you assume God's uniqueness makes Him distant or incomparable. Does this lesson challenge you to rethink how you relate to His attributes? How might you acknowledge His transcendence without using negative descriptions as a shield from responsibility?
?
If a negative attribute cannot be a cause for a positive reality, then your own avoidance of sin (a negative) does not guarantee righteousness (a positive). What concrete positive actions does this truth call you to today?

The Proof from Life and Its Unsound Premises
Presents a proof from life and opposites, then states it depends on unsound premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a rational argument used to prove God's attributes of hearing and sight, and why it depends on premises that are not fully sound.

Core Idea
A proof for God's hearing and sight from life and opposites rests on shaky premises.

Some theologians attempted to prove that Allah is Hearing and Seeing by arguing that every living being must have these attributes or their deficiencies, and since Allah is perfect, He must possess them. However, this line of reasoning, as al-Sanusi points out, hinges on premises that are not all sound. In this lesson, we examine both the argument and its critical evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage, noting the logical steps from 'God is alive' to 'He must be Hearing and Seeing'.
2
Identify each premise
The argument has at least four premises. List them as you follow the reasoning.
The commentary mentions a fourth premise as possibly sound.
3
Understand the critique
The text says the argument depends on unsound premises. The commentary clarifies that only the fourth might be sound. Consider why the others are weak.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows that not all rational proofs for divine attributes are valid; each premise must be examined.
Kalam requires rigorous logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد احتج عليه بعض الأصحاب بأنه تعالى وكل حي اتصافه بالسمع والبصر ومن صح اتصافه بصفة اتصف بها أو بضدها وضد السمع والبصر هو الصمم والعمى وإنهما من صفات النقص فامتنع اتصافه تعالى بهما فوجب اتصافه بالسمع ويتوقف هذا الاحتجاج على مقدمات لا صحة لها. قوله : ( على مقدمات لا صحة لها أي لجميعها بل الصحة إنما هي لبعضها، وهي الرابعة فتأمل .
And some of the associates argued against it that He, exalted, and every living being, its description by hearing and sight [is possible]. And whoever is validly described by an attribute, he is described by it or by its opposite. And the opposite of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness, and they are attributes of deficiency. So it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be described by them, so it is necessary that He be described by hearing. And this argument depends on premises that are not sound. His statement: (on premises that are not sound) meaning, [not sound] for all of them, but soundness is only for some of them, namely the fourth; so reflect.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents an argument used by some scholars to prove that God is Hearing and Seeing. The argument goes: God is alive (hayy). Every living being can be described by hearing and sight. If something can be described by an attribute, it must be described either by that attribute or by its opposite. The opposites of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness, which are imperfections. Since God is perfect, He cannot be described by deafness or blindness, so He must be described by hearing and sight. The text then states that this argument depends on four premises that are unsound. The commentary adds that the unsoundness applies to all premises except possibly the fourth, and calls for reflection.

Key Terms
احتجاج (ihtijaj)
الصمم (samam)
العمى ('ama)
صفات النقص (sifat al-naqs)
مقدمات (muqaddimat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof that God is Hearing and Seeing from the fact that He is alive is logically sound.
Correction: The text explicitly states that this argument depends on unsound premises, meaning it is not a valid proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the argument that God must be described by hearing and sight because He is alive and the opposite is imperfection depends on how many premises?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The proof from life and opposites for God's hearing and sight is not considered sound because it relies on premises that are not all valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refutation of the First Two Premises: Life and Opposites
Refutes the first two premises of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first two premises of the proof from life and opposites are flawed: the dissimilarity of divine life to human life and the distinction between opposites and absence of faculty.

Core Idea
God's life is unique; lacking hearing does not imply deafness.

The proof from life and opposites relies on assumptions about God's attributes. The first premise claims God's life must be like human life, enabling hearing and sight. The second premise treats deafness and blindness as opposites of those faculties. Both are rejected on rational grounds, as we will see in the following passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the first premise and its refutation
Read carefully how the author presents the claim that God's life is like ours, and then the refutation pointing out the difference and the implications.
Notice that the refutation uses examples of attributes that apply to humans but not to God.
2
Understand why God's life is different
The refutation argues that if God's life were like ours, it would allow ignorance and desire, which is impossible for God.
This shows that divine life is unique and does not necessitate hearing and sight.
3
Read the second premise and its refutation
The second premise claims deafness and blindness are opposites of hearing and sight. The refutation clarifies they are absences of faculty.
Pay attention to the key term 'عدم ملكة' (absence of faculty).
4
Grasp the concept of absence of faculty
Lacking a faculty is not the same as having its opposite. For example, a stone lacks hearing but is not deaf.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the refutation.

علينا بسبب حياتنا. المقدمة الثانية: إن الصمم والعمى ضدان لهما، وهو أيضاً ممنوع بل هما عدم ملكة لهما فلا يلزم من خلوه عن السمع والبصر اتصافه بهما لجواز انتفاء القابلية رأسا و أما اتصافه بعدمهما مع انتفاء القابلية فإنه ليس نقصاً عندنا كيف وهو أول المسألة المتنازع فيها بيننا. المقدمة الثالثة : إن المحل لا يخلو عن الشيء وضده وهو دعوى بلا دليل عليها وقد تقدم ضعفه بأن الهواء خال عن الألوان والطعوم المتضادة كلها. المقدمة الرابعة : إنه تعالى منزه عن النقائص كلها والعمدة في إثباته الإجماع على أن ساحة عزته مبرأة عن شوائب النقص وحينئذ فليعول عليه أي على الإجماع في هذه المسألة ابتداء. إذ قد أطبقوا كون هذه المقدمة ثالثة والمقدمة التي قبلها ثانية باعتبار الوقوع في أصل الاستدلال بل في البيان الذي أورده وذلك لأن الترتيب في أصل الاستدلال على عكس ما أورده فإن ما جعله مقدمة ثانية مأخوذة من قوله : في الاستدلال وضد السمع والبصر ، الصمم والعمى وما جعله مقدمة ثالثة مأخوذ من قوله : فيما قبله ومن صح بصفة اتصف بها أو بضدها. قوله : ( والعمدة في إثباته الإجماع قال في شرح المقاصد : جوابه المنع إذ ربما لا يلاحظ الإجماع عليه أو لا يراه حجة أصلا أو يعتقد أنه لا يصح في مثل هذا المطلوب التمسك به وبسائر الأدلة السمعية لكون إنزال الكتب وإرسال الرسل فرع كون الباري تعالى حياً سميعاً بصيراً وقد يعترض على الجواب
