The first: That He is alive with a life like our life that makes valid the attribution of hearing and sight. And it is rejected because His life is different from the life of others, so it is not necessary that it makes valid that attribution. And for this reason, ignorance, conjecture, desire, and aversion are not valid for Him due to His life, while they are valid for us due to our life. The second premise: That deafness and blindness are opposites of them [hearing and sight]. It is also rejected; rather they are absence of the faculty [of hearing and sight], so it does not follow from His being free from hearing and sight that He is described by them, because it is possible that the capacity is completely absent. And as for His being described by the absence of them while the capacity is absent, it is not a deficiency in our view; indeed, it is the very first point of dispute between us. The third premise: That a locus cannot be free from a thing and its opposite. This is a claim without proof; its weakness has already been presented in that air is free from all opposing colors and tastes. The fourth premise: That He, exalted, is free from all deficiencies, and the foundation for proving that is consensus (ijma') that the court of His might is free from traces of deficiency. In that case, let one rely upon it, i.e., upon consensus, in this issue from the beginning. For they have concurred that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing. And if they suffice with consensus, they relieve [us] of the burden of the other premises. How so? The authority of consensus indicating freedom from deficiency, if we establish it by the apparent meanings of the verses and hadiths which... His statement: (The third premise: that the locus is not free, etc.) — This premise being third and the one before it being second is not in terms of their occurrence in the original proof but in the exposition that he presented. That is because the order in the original proof is the reverse of what he presented. For what he made the second premise is taken from his statement in the proof: 'and the opposite of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness,' and what he made the third premise is taken from his statement earlier: 'and whoever is validly described by an attribute, he is described by it or by its opposite.' His statement: (And the foundation for proving it is consensus) — He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: The answer to it is rejection, for perhaps one does not observe consensus on it, or does not consider it a proof at all, or believes that it is not valid to rely on it in such a claim, and on all other scriptural evidences because the sending down of books and the sending of messengers is a branch of the fact that the Creator is alive, hearing, seeing. And an objection may be raised against the answer: that the author did not say there is no way except consensus, so that the mentioned answer would be pertinent; rather he said that the foundation is consensus. And it can be refuted by saying that the intention of the answerer is to deny that the foundation is only consensus, as is required by the context of the author's words. His statement: (How so? The authority of consensus, etc.) — That is, how can one rely on it? And that reliance is not free from flaw. This is what the apparent meaning of the commentator's words at the end of the discourse requires: 'There is no need for establishing hearing and sight to rely on consensus, etc.' And the more appropriate interpretation of his statement 'How so? The authority of consensus indicating freedom from deficiency' is to direct the speech as follows: Relying on consensus from the outset involves two things: one is disregarding the premise established by consensus, and the other is relying on consensus. And his statement 'how so' refers to the first, i.e., why not disregard that premise established by consensus? And the authority of consensus, etc. And as for his statement 'there is no need' contradicts establishing hearing and sight to...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage systematically refutes the four premises of the proof presented earlier. The first premise claims that God's life is like ours, making hearing and sight applicable; it is rejected because God's life is different, and many human attributes like ignorance and desire do not apply to Him. The second premise claims deafness and blindness are opposites of hearing and sight; it is refuted by saying they are absences of faculty, so lacking hearing does not imply having deafness if the capacity is absent. The third premise claims a locus cannot be free from a thing and its opposite; this is proven false by the example of air being free from colors and tastes. The fourth premise claims God is free from deficiencies, proven by consensus; the text then discusses the authority of consensus, noting that it is not a universal proof and that it may be circular because the scriptural evidence for consensus itself depends on God being hearing and seeing. The commentary further explains the ordering of the premises and the issues with relying on consensus.

Key Terms
صمم (samam)
عمى ('ama)
عدم ملكة ('adam mulka)
قابلية (qabiliyya)
حي (hayy)
حياة (haya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God lacks hearing, He must be deaf.
Correction: Deafness is the absence of hearing when the capacity for hearing exists. God lacks the capacity entirely, so His state is not considered deafness or a deficiency.

Checkpoint
What reason is given to reject the first premise?
A) God's life does not allow for ignorance and desire.
B) God cannot be described by hearing and sight.
C) The premise is based on consensus.
D) The premise is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The first two premises fail because they incorrectly analogize divine and human attributes and misunderstand the nature of absence of faculties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes, do you unconsciously imagine them as scaled-up versions of your own? This passage reveals that His life is fundamentally different—how will you train your mind to avoid this illicit analogy in your worship and understanding of Allah?
?
The refutation argues that the absence of a faculty is not a deficiency if the capacity is absent. Consider your own judgments about God's actions or attributes: Are there times you have accused Allah of lacking something because He did not act as you expected? How will you repent from such implicit presumptions and submit to His absolute perfection?
?
This lesson challenges you to examine the very first point of dispute: whether an absence of attributes in God is a deficiency. Do you believe that God's perfection is defined by what He has or by what He is in His essence? How will you adjust your understanding of divine attributes starting today?

Refutation of the Third and Fourth Premises: Locus and Consensus
Refutes the third and fourth premises and discusses the role of consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third premise (a locus cannot be free from a thing and its opposite) is false, and the difficulties with relying on consensus as a proof for God's attributes.

Core Idea
The third premise is disproven by the example of air; the fourth premise's reliance on consensus is problematic and circular.

After rejecting the first two premises, the refutation continues with the third premise that a locus cannot be free from opposing attributes. This is countered with a simple counterexample: air is free from all colors and tastes. The fourth premise appeals to consensus, but the text examines the authority of consensus and questions whether it is a valid proof, especially given the potential circularity of using scriptural evidence to prove the attributes that the scriptures themselves presuppose.

Guided Path
1
Read the third premise and its refutation
The claim that a locus must have either a thing or its opposite is refuted by the example of air being free from all colors and tastes.
This is a direct counterexample showing the premise is false.
2
Understand the significance of the counterexample
If air can be free from opposing qualities, then God can be free from hearing/sight and their supposed opposites without deficiency.
This refutes the notion that God must be described by one of the two.
3
Read the fourth premise and the discussion on consensus
The fourth premise claims God is free from all deficiencies, proven by consensus. The text then discusses the validity of consensus.
Pay attention to the objection from Sharh al-Maqasid about the circularity.
4
Consider the objection and response
The objection states that consensus may not be a valid proof, and its authority depends on scriptures that themselves assume God's hearing and sight.
This shows the difficulty of using consensus as a foundation.

The first: That He is alive with a life like our life that makes valid the attribution of hearing and sight. And it is rejected because His life is different from the life of others, so it is not necessary that it makes valid that attribution. And for this reason, ignorance, conjecture, desire, and aversion are not valid for Him due to His life, while they are valid for us due to our life. The second premise: That deafness and blindness are opposites of them [hearing and sight]. It is also rejected; rather they are absence of the faculty [of hearing and sight], so it does not follow from His being free from hearing and sight that He is described by them, because it is possible that the capacity is completely absent. And as for His being described by the absence of them while the capacity is absent, it is not a deficiency in our view; indeed, it is the very first point of dispute between us. The third premise: That a locus cannot be free from a thing and its opposite. This is a claim without proof; its weakness has already been presented in that air is free from all opposing colors and tastes. The fourth premise: That He, exalted, is free from all deficiencies, and the foundation for proving that is consensus (ijma') that the court of His might is free from traces of deficiency. In that case, let one rely upon it, i.e., upon consensus, in this issue from the beginning. For they have concurred that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing. And if they suffice with consensus, they relieve [us] of the burden of the other premises. How so? The authority of consensus indicating freedom from deficiency, if we establish it by the apparent meanings of the verses and hadiths which... His statement: (The third premise: that the locus is not free, etc.) — This premise being third and the one before it being second is not in terms of their occurrence in the original proof but in the exposition that he presented. That is because the order in the original proof is the reverse of what he presented. For what he made the second premise is taken from his statement in the proof: 'and the opposite of hearing and sight are deafness and blindness,' and what he made the third premise is taken from his statement earlier: 'and whoever is validly described by an attribute, he is described by it or by its opposite.' His statement: (And the foundation for proving it is consensus) — He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: The answer to it is rejection, for perhaps one does not observe consensus on it, or does not consider it a proof at all, or believes that it is not valid to rely on it in such a claim, and on all other scriptural evidences because the sending down of books and the sending of messengers is a branch of the fact that the Creator is alive, hearing, seeing. And an objection may be raised against the answer: that the author did not say there is no way except consensus, so that the mentioned answer would be pertinent; rather he said that the foundation is consensus. And it can be refuted by saying that the intention of the answerer is to deny that the foundation is only consensus, as is required by the context of the author's words. His statement: (How so? The authority of consensus, etc.) — That is, how can one rely on it? And that reliance is not free from flaw. This is what the apparent meaning of the commentator's words at the end of the discourse requires: 'There is no need for establishing hearing and sight to rely on consensus, etc.' And the more appropriate interpretation of his statement 'How so? The authority of consensus indicating freedom from deficiency' is to direct the speech as follows: Relying on consensus from the outset involves two things: one is disregarding the premise established by consensus, and the other is relying on consensus. And his statement 'how so' refers to the first, i.e., why not disregard that premise established by consensus? And the authority of consensus, etc. And as for his statement 'there is no need' contradicts establishing hearing and sight to...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage systematically refutes the four premises of the proof presented earlier. The first premise claims that God's life is like ours, making hearing and sight applicable; it is rejected because God's life is different, and many human attributes like ignorance and desire do not apply to Him. The second premise claims deafness and blindness are opposites of hearing and sight; it is refuted by saying they are absences of faculty, so lacking hearing does not imply having deafness if the capacity is absent. The third premise claims a locus cannot be free from a thing and its opposite; this is proven false by the example of air being free from colors and tastes. The fourth premise claims God is free from deficiencies, proven by consensus; the text then discusses the authority of consensus, noting that it is not a universal proof and that it may be circular because the scriptural evidence for consensus itself depends on God being hearing and seeing. The commentary further explains the ordering of the premises and the issues with relying on consensus.

Key Terms
محل (mahall)
إجماع (ijma')
شرح المقاصد (Sharh al-Maqasid)
عدمية ('adamiyya)
نقيض (naqid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is an unquestionable proof for God's attributes.
Correction: The text shows that consensus may not be accepted as a proof, and even if it is, its authority may depend on scriptural evidence that itself relies on the attributes being proven.

Checkpoint
What example is used to refute the third premise?
A) The absence of color in water.
B) Air being free from all colors and tastes.
C) The impossibility of a vacuum.
D) The existence of deafness.

Key Takeaway
The third premise is empirically false, and the fourth premise's reliance on consensus is problematic due to potential circularity and lack of universal acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The third premise seemed logical but was proven false by a simple example. What is one belief you hold about Allah that you think is logically necessary, but have you ever honestly considered it might be based on a flawed premise? What evidence would you need to test it?
?
The fourth premise relies on consensus, but the passage shows consensus can be circular. When you accept a teaching about Allah's attributes from your community, do you do so because of consensus alone, or have you personally examined the primary sources? How confident are you that your belief is not just inherited?
?
The circularity argument reveals that proving Allah's attributes requires a deeper foundation. If you had to defend why Allah is Hearing and Seeing to someone who rejects revelation, what would you say? Are you relying on an argument that presupposes the very thing you are proving?

Refuting the First Proposition on Divine Hearing and Sight
Refutation of the first proposition on divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between human life and divine life does not necessitate that Allah possesses hearing and sight.

Core Idea
Allah's life is unlike ours, so it does not imply the same attributes.

In this passage, the commentator refutes the first proposition that attempts to prove Allah's hearing and sight by analogy to human life. He argues that Allah's life is fundamentally different from created life, and therefore the analogy fails. We will examine this refutation closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text for the first proposition and its refutation, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: the proposition is stated, then rejected.
2
Identify the proposition being refuted
The proposition claims that Allah is living with a life similar to ours, which qualifies Him for hearing and sight. Notice the assumed similarity.
3
Understand the commentator's argument
The commentator rejects the analogy by stating that Allah's life is different from others' lives, so it does not have to result in the same attributes. He gives an example: human life leads to ignorance, opinion, desire, and aversion, but these are impossible for Allah.
This shows that the attributes resulting from human life do not apply to Allah, so the analogy is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد السادس في أنه تعالى سميع بصير ( الأولى : أنه حي بحياة مثل حياتنا المصححة للاتصاف بالسمع والبصر ( وإنه ممنوع إذ حياته مخالفة لحياة غيره ) فلا يجب كونها مصححة لذلك الاتصاف ) ولهذا لا يصح عليه ) بسبب حياته ( الجهل والظن والشهوة والنفرة مع صحتها علينا بسبب حياتنا المقدمة ( الثانية: إن الصمم والعمى ضدان لهما، وهو) أيضاً ( ممنوع بل) هما ) عدم ملكة (لهما فلا يلزم من خلوه عن السمع والبصر اتصافه بهما لجواز انتفاء القابلية رأسا ( و ) أما ( اتصافه بعدمهما ) مع انتفاء القابلية فإنه (ليس نقصاً) عندنا كيف ( وهو أول المسألة ( المتنازع فيها بيننا .
The Fourth Observatory - The Sixth Objective: That He, exalted, is Hearing and Seeing. (The first: that He is living with a life like our life that qualifies for the description of hearing and sight. (And it is rejected because His life is different from the life of others) so it is not necessary that it qualifies for that description. (And for this reason) due to His life (ignorance, opinion, desire, and aversion are not attributed to Him) while they are attributed to us due to our life. The second: that deafness and blindness are opposites to them, and it is also rejected, rather they are privation of the faculty, so the absence of hearing and sight does not necessitate that He is described with them, because it is possible that the capacity is absolutely absent. (And) as for (His being described with their absence) with the absence of capacity, it is not a deficiency according to us. How? (That is the very first point of the matter under dispute between us.)
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first two propositions (muqaddimat) that some might use to argue that Allah must be described with hearing and sight by analogy to human attributes. The first proposition claims that Allah is living with a life similar to ours, which qualifies Him for hearing and sight. The commentator rejects this by noting that Allah's life is fundamentally different from that of created beings; consequently, it does not necessitate the same attributes. He illustrates this by pointing out that our life allows for ignorance, opinion, desire, and aversion, while these are impossible for Allah despite His life. The second proposition asserts that deafness and blindness are opposites of hearing and sight, so if Allah lacks these, He must possess hearing and sight. The commentator rejects this as well, explaining that deafness and blindness are not actual opposites but rather the privation (ʿadam milkah) of the faculty of hearing or sight. The absence of a faculty does not imply the presence of its opposite; it may simply be that the subject is not capable of possessing that faculty at all. Thus, the lack of hearing and sight in Allah does not entail that He has their opposites, and His being free from them is not a deficiency—this is precisely the point under dispute.

سمع
بصر
ممنوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that Allah's life must be exactly like ours and therefore necessitate the same attributes.
Correction: Allah's life is unique and does not entail the same attributes as human life.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the first proposition that Allah is living with a life like ours, qualifying Him for hearing and sight?
A) Because Allah's life is different from ours, so it does not necessitate the same attributes.
B) Because Allah does not have life at all.
C) Because hearing and sight are not perfections.
D) Because the proposition is not from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Allah's life is unlike creation, so the analogy from human attributes to divine attributes is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever imagine Allah as hearing and seeing like a human, with human-like attention and understanding? How must you reshape your understanding after learning that His life is completely unlike yours?
?
The text shows that because of our life, we have ignorance, desire, and aversion. In what ways do these attributes of yours distort your worship and your concept of Allah? What specific step will you take today to begin seeing Allah’s life as truly transcendent?
?
If you truly believed that Allah’s life is categorically different from yours, how would it change the way you speak about Him, beseech Him, and trust in His decree? Are you willing to uproot any casual anthropomorphic language from your tongue starting now?

Refuting the Second Proposition on Divine Hearing and Sight
Refutation of the second proposition on divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of opposites does not prove the existence of hearing and sight in Allah, and why it is not a deficiency.

Core Idea
Deafness and blindness are privations, not opposites, so lack of hearing doesn't imply their presence.

The commentator now addresses the second proposition, which argues that if Allah lacks the opposites of hearing and sight (i.e., not deaf/blind), then He must possess them. The commentator rejects this by explaining that deafness and blindness are not true opposites but the absence of the faculty. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second proposition
Read the Arabic text and translation. Focus on the claim and its refutation.
2
Identify the claim in the second proposition
The proposition states that deafness and blindness are opposites of hearing and sight, so if Allah is free from them, He must possess hearing and sight.
3
Understand the refutation
The commentator rejects the claim by asserting that deafness and blindness are not true opposites but privation (ʿadam milkah) of the faculty. Therefore, the absence of hearing and sight does not necessitate their existence; it could be that the subject lacks the capacity altogether. Moreover, being free from these privations with no capacity is not a deficiency.
This shifts the discussion to the nature of the attributes and whether lack of hearing is a deficiency when the subject is not capable of hearing.

Source Text

?

Will you let this lesson deepen your awe and remove any trace of anthropomorphism from your heart?

The Third and Fourth Propositions
The commentator introduces and refutes the third proposition, then states the fourth proposition and the initial reliance on consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third proposition (that a locus cannot be free from a thing and its opposite) is rejected and how the fourth proposition relies on consensus as a proof for Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
A locus can be free from opposites, so Allah's attributes are not forced.

In this passage, the author addresses two key propositions from the opponents' argument regarding Allah's attributes of hearing and sight. The third proposition falsely assumes a necessary polarity, while the fourth appeals to consensus. Let's see how these are examined.

Guided Path
1
Read the propositions
Carefully read the Arabic passage introducing the third and fourth propositions. Note the claim that a locus must have a thing or its opposite.
2
Understand the refutation of the third
The author dismisses the third proposition as an unsubstantiated claim, providing the example of air being free from colors and tastes.
This shows a locus can be free from opposites.
3
Identify the fourth proposition
The fourth proposition emphasizes Allah's transcendence and presents consensus as the main proof.
4
Note the initial reliance on consensus
The author initially advises relying on consensus for this matter, as it spares the trouble of other propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : إن المحل لا يخلو عن الشيء وضده وهو دعوى بلا دليل عليها ( وقد تقدم ضعفه ) بأن الهواء خال عن الألوان والطعوم المتضادة كلها . المقدمة الرابعة : إنه تعالى منزه عن النقائص كلها والعمدة في إثباته الإجماع على أن ساحة عزته مبرأة عن شوائب النقص وحينئذ ( فليعول عليه ) أي على الإجماع ( في هذه المسألة ابتداء ). إذ قد أطبقوا على أنه تعالى سميع بصير (و) إذا اكتفوا بالإجماع ( يكفون مؤونة سائر المقدمات. كيف وحجية الإجماع ( الدال على التنزه ( إن أثبتناها بالظواهر من الآيات والأحاديث التي تدل على حجية الإجماع ( فالظواهر الدالة على السمع والبصر أقوى منها ) أي من الظواهر الدالة على حجية الإجماع، إذ يتجه على هذه اعتراضات كثيرة يحتاج إلى دفعها فلا معنى للعدول عما هو أقوى في إثبات المدعى إلى التمسك بشيء يحتاج في إثباته إلى ما هو أضعف لأنه تطويل للمسافة مع التشبث بالأضعف ) وإن أثبتناها ) أي حجية الإجماع ( بالعلم الضروري من الدين فذلك العلم الضروري (ثابت في المسألة ) التي نحن فيها ( سواء بسواء ) فلا حاجة بنا في إثبات السمع والبصر إلى التمسك بالإجماع ثم التمسك في حجيته بالعلم الضروري فإنه تطويل بلا طائل، بل نقول ابتداء : هو مما علم من الدين بالضرورة كما ذكرناه )
The third proposition: that a locus is not free from a thing and its opposite. This is a claim without proof. (Its weakness has already been shown) because air is free from all opposite colors and flavors. The fourth proposition: that He, exalted, is free from all deficiencies, and the mainstay in proving it is the consensus that the court of His might is purified from stains of deficiency. Then (rely upon it) i.e., upon consensus (in this matter from the beginning). For they have agreed that He, exalted, is Hearing and Seeing. (And) if they are content with consensus, (they are spared the trouble of the other propositions. How? And the authority of consensus (indicating transcendence) if we establish it by the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of the verses and hadiths that indicate the authority of consensus (then the apparent meanings indicating hearing and sight are stronger than them) i.e., than the apparent meanings indicating the authority of consensus, because many objections arise against these that need to be refuted, so there is no meaning in turning from what is stronger in establishing the claim to adhering to something whose proof is weaker, because it is lengthening the distance while clinging to the weaker. And if we establish it i.e., the authority of consensus (by necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) from religion, then that necessary knowledge is established in the matter under discussion equally) so we have no need in proving hearing and sight to adhere to consensus and then adhere to its authority by necessary knowledge, for it is useless lengthening. Rather we say from the beginning: it is known from religion by necessity as we mentioned.)
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage refutes the third and fourth propositions. The third proposition claims that a locus (maḥall) cannot be free from a thing and its opposite—meaning that if Allah does not have hearing and sight, He must have their opposites, deafness and blindness. The commentator dismisses this as an unsubstantiated claim, citing the example of air, which is free from all opposite colors and flavors. The fourth proposition asserts that Allah is free from all deficiencies, and that consensus (ijmāʿ) is the main proof for His hearing and sight. The commentator notes that if consensus is taken as the primary evidence, it should suffice for establishing the attribute. However, he then critically examines the value of consensus. If consensus itself is proven by apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of scripture, those scriptural proofs for hearing and sight are stronger than the proofs for consensus, because the latter face many objections. If consensus is proven by necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) from religion, then that same necessary knowledge directly affirms hearing and sight, making the appeal to consensus redundant. Thus, the commentator concludes that the proper approach is to rely directly on what is known from religion by necessity, without the detour through consensus.

Key Terms
محل
ضد
هواء
ألوان
طعوم
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something lacks an attribute, it must have the opposite attribute.
Correction: Absence of a quality does not imply its opposite; air has no color or taste, so it is simply free.

Checkpoint
What example does the commentator use to refute the third proposition?
A) A stone
B) Air
C) Water
D) Fire

Key Takeaway
The third proposition is invalid because a locus can be free from opposites, as shown by air.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that Allah is free from deficiency, but have you ever doubted that consensus is enough as proof? Where do you seek certainty—through intellectual proofs or through the collective testimony of the scholars? Examine your heart: do you trust the community of believers enough to rely on their consensus as a foundation for your faith?
?
The opponents assumed that every locus must contain one of two opposites. Where in your life have you assumed that if you lack a virtue, you must have its corresponding vice? How does the example of air challenge that assumption, and what false judgments about yourself or others can you now correct?
?
The author dismissed the third proposition as a claim without proof. Are you holding onto beliefs about Allah that have no proof, simply because they seem logical? Will you dare to let go of those assumptions and accept only what is established by clear evidence or consensus?

The Superiority of Direct Evidence over Consensus
The commentator examines the evidential value of consensus and argues that direct scriptural or necessary knowledge is either stronger or sufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direct scriptural evidence or necessary knowledge is superior to consensus for proving Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
Consensus is either weaker than direct evidence or redundant with necessary knowledge.

The author now critically examines the value of consensus in proving Allah's hearing and sight. He shows that whether consensus is proven by apparent meanings or necessary knowledge, it is either weaker than direct evidence or unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the analysis of consensus
Read the passage that starts with 'كيف وحجية الإجماع'. It presents two scenarios for proving consensus.
2
Understand the first scenario
If consensus is proven by ẓawāhir, then those ẓawāhir for hearing/sight are stronger, making consensus indirect and weaker.
3
Understand the second scenario
If consensus is proven by necessary knowledge, then that knowledge already establishes hearing/sight, making consensus redundant.
4
Conclusion
The author concludes that it is better to rely directly on necessary knowledge of the religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : إن المحل لا يخلو عن الشيء وضده وهو دعوى بلا دليل عليها ( وقد تقدم ضعفه ) بأن الهواء خال عن الألوان والطعوم المتضادة كلها . المقدمة الرابعة : إنه تعالى منزه عن النقائص كلها والعمدة في إثباته الإجماع على أن ساحة عزته مبرأة عن شوائب النقص وحينئذ ( فليعول عليه ) أي على الإجماع ( في هذه المسألة ابتداء ). إذ قد أطبقوا على أنه تعالى سميع بصير (و) إذا اكتفوا بالإجماع ( يكفون مؤونة سائر المقدمات. كيف وحجية الإجماع ( الدال على التنزه ( إن أثبتناها بالظواهر من الآيات والأحاديث التي تدل على حجية الإجماع ( فالظواهر الدالة على السمع والبصر أقوى منها ) أي من الظواهر الدالة على حجية الإجماع، إذ يتجه على هذه اعتراضات كثيرة يحتاج إلى دفعها فلا معنى للعدول عما هو أقوى في إثبات المدعى إلى التمسك بشيء يحتاج في إثباته إلى ما هو أضعف لأنه تطويل للمسافة مع التشبث بالأضعف ) وإن أثبتناها ) أي حجية الإجماع ( بالعلم الضروري من الدين فذلك العلم الضروري (ثابت في المسألة ) التي نحن فيها ( سواء بسواء ) فلا حاجة بنا في إثبات السمع والبصر إلى التمسك بالإجماع ثم التمسك في حجيته بالعلم الضروري فإنه تطويل بلا طائل، بل نقول ابتداء : هو مما علم من الدين بالضرورة كما ذكرناه )
The third proposition: that a locus is not free from a thing and its opposite. This is a claim without proof. (Its weakness has already been shown) because air is free from all opposite colors and flavors. The fourth proposition: that He, exalted, is free from all deficiencies, and the mainstay in proving it is the consensus that the court of His might is purified from stains of deficiency. Then (rely upon it) i.e., upon consensus (in this matter from the beginning). For they have agreed that He, exalted, is Hearing and Seeing. (And) if they are content with consensus, (they are spared the trouble of the other propositions. How? And the authority of consensus (indicating transcendence) if we establish it by the apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of the verses and hadiths that indicate the authority of consensus (then the apparent meanings indicating hearing and sight are stronger than them) i.e., than the apparent meanings indicating the authority of consensus, because many objections arise against these that need to be refuted, so there is no meaning in turning from what is stronger in establishing the claim to adhering to something whose proof is weaker, because it is lengthening the distance while clinging to the weaker. And if we establish it i.e., the authority of consensus (by necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) from religion, then that necessary knowledge is established in the matter under discussion equally) so we have no need in proving hearing and sight to adhere to consensus and then adhere to its authority by necessary knowledge, for it is useless lengthening. Rather we say from the beginning: it is known from religion by necessity as we mentioned.)
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage refutes the third and fourth propositions. The third proposition claims that a locus (maḥall) cannot be free from a thing and its opposite—meaning that if Allah does not have hearing and sight, He must have their opposites, deafness and blindness. The commentator dismisses this as an unsubstantiated claim, citing the example of air, which is free from all opposite colors and flavors. The fourth proposition asserts that Allah is free from all deficiencies, and that consensus (ijmāʿ) is the main proof for His hearing and sight. The commentator notes that if consensus is taken as the primary evidence, it should suffice for establishing the attribute. However, he then critically examines the value of consensus. If consensus itself is proven by apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) of scripture, those scriptural proofs for hearing and sight are stronger than the proofs for consensus, because the latter face many objections. If consensus is proven by necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) from religion, then that same necessary knowledge directly affirms hearing and sight, making the appeal to consensus redundant. Thus, the commentator concludes that the proper approach is to rely directly on what is known from religion by necessity, without the detour through consensus.

Key Terms
إجماع
ظواهر
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is the strongest proof for divine attributes.
Correction: Direct scriptural evidence and necessary knowledge are either stronger or sufficient, making consensus secondary.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, if consensus is proven by necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī), what follows?
A) Consensus becomes the strongest proof.
B) Necessary knowledge also directly proves hearing and sight, making consensus redundant.
C) Consensus is still needed to confirm the attribute.
D) Necessary knowledge is insufficient for proving attributes.

Key Takeaway
Consensus is not the best proof for Allah's attributes; direct evidence from scripture or necessary knowledge is either stronger or sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about Allah, do you check whether your evidence is truly strong and direct, or do you rely on what others commonly accept? What would it take for you to shift to a directly grounded conviction?
?
The author calls relying on weaker evidence 'useless lengthening.' In your spiritual life, are there any practices or convictions that have become unnecessary detours—relying on secondary sources when you could go straight to revelation?
?
Are you willing to reexamine a belief you've held because it is popular, even if the evidence for it is weaker than the evidence for another view? What might be holding you back from that examination?

Refuting the Identity of Perception and Knowledge
Refutation of the identity of perception and knowledge using intuitive difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the intuitive argument against equating perception (hearing, sight) with knowledge and why perception involves an extra element beyond mere knowledge.

Core Idea
Perception adds a distinct element beyond knowledge; we sense it intuitively.

Before discussing the divine attributes of hearing and sight, we must first understand how perception relates to knowledge. Some theologians claimed that perception is identical to knowledge. This lesson presents a simple yet powerful refutation based on our own experience: when we know something and then perceive it, we feel an added quality. The commentator explains this argument and notes that the author of the Mawaqif offered a critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage that presents the argument against identity. Then read its literal translation.
Notice the phrase 'نفس العلم' (identical to knowledge) and the intuitive contrast.
2
Grasp the intuitive argument
The argument says: when you know something completely and then see it, you intuitively sense a difference. The second state has 'more' – that extra is sight.
This is a 'by al-badīha' (intuitive) proof, meant to be self-evident.
3
Understand the opponent's view
The opponents (a group) claim that hearing and sight are themselves knowledge of what is heard/seen when they occur, so they are accidents and not additional attributes.
Their position reduces perception to a temporal mode of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : قد تقدم) في مباحث العلم ( أن طائفة يزعمون أن الإدراك أعني السمع والبصر وسائر أخواتهما ( نفس العلم) بمتعلقه الذي هو المدرك ( وقد أبطلناه ) بأنا إذا علمنا شيئاً علماً تاماً جليا ثم أبصرناه فإنا نجد بالبديهة بين الحالتين فرقا ونعلم بالضرورة أن الحالة الثانية تشتمل على أمر زائد مع حصول العلم فيهما فذلك الزائد هو الإبصار، وللمصنف في هذا الإبطال مناقشة قد مرت هناك . ( فهؤلاء زعموا أن السمع والبصر نفس العلم بالمسموع والمبصر عند حدوثهما فيكونان (حادثين وراجعين إلى العلم لا صفتين زائدتين عليه. وفي المحصل اتفق المسلمون على أنه تعالى سميع بصير لكنهم اختلفوا في معناه فقالت الفلاسفة والكعبي وأبو الحسين البصري: ذلك عبارة عن علمه تعالى بالمسموعات والمبصرات وقال الجمهور منا ومن المعتزلة والكرامية : إنهما صفتان زائدتان على العلم. وقال ناقده : أراد فلاسفة الإسلام فإن وصفه تعالى قوله: (مناقشة) هي أنه لم يجوز أن يكون تفارق الحالتين بالهوية، وأما تفارقهما بالحقيقة فلا وجه لتجويزه وإن كان كلام المصنف فيما سبق مشعراً به وذلك لأنه إذا سلم اختلاف أنواع التعقل فالظاهر أن مرجعها مختلف فلا يكون العلم صفة واحدة كما ذهبوا إليه كيف لا؟ ولو جوز تنوع الآثار مع وحدة المنشأ يثبت تنوع الصفات كما ستطلع عليه في أثناء بحث الكلام. قوله : ( نفس العلم بالمسموع والمبصر) لا يريدون بذلك نفي الانكشاف وراء الانكشاف العلمي الحاصل قبل حدوث المسموع والمبصر، بل يثبتون الانكشاف التام الذي يثبته غيرهم لكنهم يقولون : هذا الانكشاف أيضا عائد إلى تعلق العلم على وجه مخصوص فلا يحتاج لذلك إلى صفة زائدة غيره، فللعلم عندهم صفتان من التعلق : تعلق قبل حدوث المسموع والمبصر وبه يحصل الانكشاف العلمي المعروف، وتعلق حال حدوثهما وبه يحصل ذلك الانكشاف التام .
Note: It has already been stated in the discussions of knowledge that a group claim that perception (idrāk), meaning hearing, sight, and the rest of their siblings, is identical to knowledge of its object, which is the perceived. (And we have refuted it) by the fact that if we know something with complete and clear knowledge and then we see it, we find intuitively a difference between the two states and we know necessarily that the second state includes an additional matter while knowledge is present in both, so that additional element is sight. And the author has a critique of this refutation that has passed there. (So these claimed that hearing and sight are identical to knowledge of what is heard and what is seen at the time of their occurrence, so they are accidents (ḥādithān) and revert to knowledge, not two additional attributes. And in al-Muḥaṣṣal, the Muslims agreed that He is Hearing and Seeing, but they differed in its meaning: the philosophers, al-Kaʿbī, and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī said: that is an expression of His knowledge of what is heard and what is seen. And the majority of us and of the Muʿtazila and Karramiyya said: they are two attributes additional to knowledge. And the critic said: he meant the philosophers of Islam, for His description— His saying: (Critique) is that he did not allow that the two states differ by identity, but as for their differing in reality, there is no basis for allowing it, even though the author's earlier speech hints at it, and that is because if the diversity of types of intellection is admitted, then apparently their referents are different, so knowledge would not be a single attribute as they held—how not? And if it is allowed that effects vary while the source is one, that would prove the diversity of attributes, as you will see in the discussion of speech. His saying: (identical to knowledge of what is heard and what is seen) they do not thereby mean the denial of the disclosure beyond the scientific disclosure that occurs before the occurrence of the heard and the seen, rather they affirm the complete disclosure that others affirm, but they say: this disclosure also reverts to the attachment of knowledge in a particular manner, so there is no need for an additional attribute besides it. For according to them, knowledge has two modes of attachment: attachment before the occurrence of the heard and the seen—by which the known scientific disclosure occurs—and attachment at the time of their occurrence—by which that complete disclosure occurs.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbīh) that addresses a theological debate about the nature of perception (idrāk) and its relationship to knowledge, specifically regarding the divine attributes of hearing and sight. It begins by mentioning a group who claim that perception—including hearing, sight, and similar faculties—is identical to knowledge of the perceived object. The commentator refutes this by pointing to the intuitive difference between knowing something and then perceiving it (e.g., seeing it): we find a clear difference, with perception adding an extra element beyond mere knowledge. That extra element is sight (ibṣār). The author of the Mawaqif is noted to have a critique of this refutation, which was discussed earlier. The passage then summarizes the differing opinions among Muslims on the meaning of Allah being Hearing and Seeing. According to al-Muḥaṣṣal, all Muslims agree that Allah is described as such, but they disagree on the interpretation: the philosophers, al-Kaʿbī, and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī say it is a metaphor for His knowledge of what is heard and seen; the majority of the Ashʿarites, Muʿtazilites, and Karramites hold that hearing and sight are two additional attributes distinct from knowledge. A critic (nāqid) is mentioned as saying that the philosophers meant the philosophers of Islam. The commentary then digresses to explain a previous criticism: the author did not allow that the two states (knowing and seeing) differ in identity but only in reality, but the commentator finds no basis for even allowing that difference if the diversity of types of intellection is accepted. Finally, the commentator clarifies that those who equate perception with knowledge do not deny the complete disclosure that occurs at the moment of perception; rather, they say that this disclosure is simply a specific mode of knowledge's attachment to its object, not requiring a separate attribute. According to them, knowledge has two modes of attachment: one before the object's occurrence (giving general knowledge) and one at the moment of occurrence (giving the complete disclosure). Thus, no extra attribute is necessary.

Key Terms
إدراك
علم
إبصار
حادثين
صفات زائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception and knowledge are the same thing.
Correction: The intuitive argument shows that perceiving (e.g., seeing) adds something beyond knowing—an extra element we call sight.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's refutation, what do we find when we know something completely and then see it?
A) No difference whatsoever.
B) A difference between the two states, with seeing adding an extra element.
C) That seeing is exactly the same as knowing.
D) That the object changes.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive difference between knowing and perceiving proves that perception is not identical to knowledge; it adds a distinct element.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you know something about Allah but do not actively perceive His signs in creation, what is missing in your heart that this lesson identifies as a real, additional element? How can you move from mere knowledge to true perception today?
?
Have you ever treated your sensory experiences of Allah's creation as merely intellectual exercises, forgetting that perception adds a weight that pure knowledge cannot? What will you do this week to let what you see deepen your knowledge into something more?
?
The refutation relies on an intuitive, undeniable difference between knowing and perceiving. Is there an area of your faith where you have settled for knowing without perceiving? What concrete step will you take to experience that 'additional matter'?

Differing Views on Divine Hearing and Sight
Summary of Muslim opinions on the meaning of Allah's hearing and sight, with clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main Muslim positions on the meaning of Allah's attributes of hearing and sight, and the clarification about the philosophers' view.

Core Idea
Muslims agree Allah is Hearing and Seeing, but differ: some say it's His knowledge, others add distinct attributes.

While all Muslims affirm that Allah is Hearing and Seeing, they disagree on what these attributes mean. Are they simply expressions of His all-encompassing knowledge, or are they distinct additional attributes? This lesson surveys the key positions: the philosophers and some Mu'tazila interpret them as knowledge, while the majority of Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and Karramiyya hold them to be separate attributes. A critic clarifies that by 'philosophers' the author meant the philosophers of Islam, and the passage further explains that those who equate perception with knowledge do not deny a 'complete disclosure' but attribute it to a special mode of knowledge attachment.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinions passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وفي المحصل' to the end, along with its translation.
Notice the three groups mentioned and the critic's remark.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage lists: (1) philosophers + al-Ka'bi + Abu al-Husayn al-Basri say it's His knowledge; (2) majority of Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, Karramiyya say they are additional attributes; (3) a critic says 'philosophers' means philosophers of Islam.
Also note the commentator's own explanation about the two modes of attachment.
3
Understand the 'complete disclosure'
The commentator clarifies that those who equate perception with knowledge do not deny a 'complete disclosure' beyond ordinary knowledge; they explain it as a specific attachment of knowledge.
This defends their view from the charge of denying any extra cognitive content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Muslims interpret divine hearing and sight as additional attributes distinct from knowledge.
Correction: While the majority holds that view, a significant group (including some Muʿtazila and philosophers) interprets them as expressions of Allah's knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of al-Kaʿbī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī regarding the meaning of Allah being Hearing and Seeing?
A) They are two additional attributes beyond knowledge.
B) They are expressions of His knowledge of what is heard and seen.
C) They are identical to His essence without any attributes.
D) They are temporary attributes that will cease.

Key Takeaway
The Muslim community agrees that Allah is Hearing and Seeing, but the interpretation varies: some see these as His knowledge, others as distinct attributes; a further nuance explains perception as a special mode of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that some scholars interpret Allah's hearing and sight as merely His knowledge, while others see them as distinct attributes, which view makes you feel more personally accountable for your private moments? Will you this week take one action that reflects the belief that Allah sees and hears you at all times?
?
The commentator defends the philosopher's view by saying knowledge has two attachments, including a 'complete disclosure' at the moment something happens. Do you truly live as if Allah has complete disclosure of every thought and action you take right now? If not, what particular thought or habit are you hiding that you need to surrender to His awareness today?
?
Consider the logical argument in this lesson: if the referents of intellection differ, then knowledge cannot be a single attribute. Does this challenge you to think more deeply about the nature of Allah's attributes rather than accepting comfortable labels? Will you commit to studying theological disagreements with an open heart, seeking truth even when it unsettles you?

Divine Hearing and Sight: Known from Revelation
Opens the sixth section on Allah's hearing and sight from revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attributes of hearing and sight are known exclusively through revelation, not reason, and how to properly affirm them without anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Revelation, not reason, is the source for knowing Allah's hearing and sight.

This passage opens the discussion on Allah's attributes of hearing and sight. The author makes clear that these attributes are not rationally deduced but are accepted from revelation. Moreover, he explains why other senses like taste, smell, and touch are not attributed to Allah, and he guides us on the proper theological stance: belief without anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full literal translation. Let the author's own wording set the context.
Notice the formal opening: 'المرصد الرابع - المقصد السادس'.
2
Identify the source of hearing and sight
The passage states that hearing (السمع) and sight (البصر) are derived from revelation (النقل). Mark this key point.
The author uses 'مستفاد من النقل' meaning 'derived from transmission/revelation'.
3
Note the exclusion of other senses
Allah is not described with taste, smell, or touch because no revelation has come with them. Understand the principle: attributes are only known through revelation.
4
Understand the rational limitation
The passage argues that reason alone cannot affirm attributes resembling animal senses. This shows the insufficiency of pure reason in this domain.
The phrase 'مما لا يمكن بالعقل' emphasizes impossibility by reason.
5
Learn the correct approach
The proper response is to believe by faith, affirm that they are not like human organs, and acknowledge our inability to grasp their reality.
This is a model of how to handle revealed attributes without ta'til or tashbih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد السادس : في أنه تعالى سميع بصير بالسمع والبصر مستفاد من النقل، وإنما لم يوصف بالذوق والشم واللمس لعدم ورود النقل بها. وإذا نظر في ذلك من حيث العقل لم يوجد له وجه سوى ماذكره هؤلاء فإن إثبات صفتين شبيهتين بسمع الحيوانات وبصرها مما لا يمكن بالعقل، والأولى أن يقال : لما ورد النقل بهما آمنا بذلك وعرفنا أنهما لا يكونان بالآلتين المعروفيتين، واعترفنا بعدم الوقوف على حقيقتهما
The Fourth Observatory - The Sixth Objective: That He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing. Hearing and sight are derived from revelation, and He is not described with taste, smell, or touch because no revelation has come with them. And if one considers this from the rational perspective, he finds no basis except what these [scholars] mentioned: for affirming two attributes similar to the hearing and sight of animals is not possible by reason. The best is to say: Since revelation has come with them, we believe in that and we know that they are not with the two known organs, and we acknowledge our inability to grasp their reality.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the source of knowledge for Allah's attributes of hearing and sight. It states that these are known from revelation, not reason. It explains that rational inquiry alone cannot establish such attributes because they would resemble animal senses. Therefore, the correct approach is to accept them by faith, while affirming that they are not like human senses and that we cannot fully comprehend their reality.

Al-Baqi's First Objection on Divine Hearing and Sight
Presents al-Baqi's first objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqi's first objection against divine hearing and sight and the refutation that distinguishes between human and divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are not bound by physical limitations.

In this lesson, we examine al-Baqi's first objection to Allah possessing hearing and sight. Al-Baqi argues that these would require physical affection, which is impossible for Allah. The response clarifies that human experiences do not dictate divine realities. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where al-Baqi presents his objection. Notice the key claim that hearing and sight are affections of a sense organ or conditional upon it.
2
Identify the core premise
The objection rests on an analogy from human experience. Identify that premise and how it leads to the conclusion that these attributes are impossible for Allah.
The premise is that in us, hearing and sight involve affection, so they must involve affection in Allah as well.
3
Read the refutation
Read the answer that denies the analogy. Note the two key points: (1) Concomitance does not imply identity or conditionality, and (2) divine attributes are fundamentally different from human ones.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader principle that attributes of Allah are not bound by the limitations of created beings. This refutation exemplifies a key method in kalam to avoid anthropomorphism.
This lesson shows how Islamic theology safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( احتج الباقي ) على نفيهما عنه تعالى ( بوجهين : الأول : إنهما تأثر الحاسة ) عن المسموع والمبصر ( أو مشروطان (به كسائر الإحساسات ( وأنه ( أي التأثر المذكور ( محال في حقه تعالى. (والجواب منع ذلك ) إذ المعلوم أنهما لا يحصلان لنا إلا مع التأثر ) ولا يلزم من حصولهما مقارنا للتأثر فينا كونهما نفس ذلك التأثر أو مشروطين به وإن سلمنا أنه كذلك في الشاهد فلم قلتم : إنه في الغائب كذلك فإن صفاته تعالى مخالفة بالحقيقة لصفاتنا . فجاز أن لا يكون سمعه وبصره نفس التأثر ولا مشروطاً به
Al-Baqi argued for negating them (i.e., hearing and sight) from Allah, exalted, with two aspects: The first: They are an affection of the sense organ from the heard and seen, or they are conditional upon it like other sensations, and that affection is impossible with respect to Allah. The answer is to deny that. For it is known that they do not occur for us except with affection, but their occurring concomitant with affection in us does not necessitate that they are that affection itself or conditional upon it. And even if we concede that it is so in the witnessed world, why do you say it is so in the unseen? For Allah's attributes are different in reality from our attributes. So it is possible that His hearing and sight are neither affection itself nor conditional upon it.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of al-Baqi against Allah having hearing and sight: that they would be affections of a sense organ, which is impossible for Allah. The answer refutes this by distinguishing between our experience and Allah's. Even if in us hearing and sight involve affection, it does not follow that they are identical to that affection or conditional upon it. Moreover, attributes of Allah are unique and not analogous to ours. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
احتج الباقي
تأثر
الحاسة
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Allah's hearing and sight must involve physical organs because human hearing and sight do.
Correction: The refutation shows that divine attributes are not bound by human limitations; they are of a different reality.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of al-Baqi's first objection?
A) Hearing and sight are accidental
B) They require a sense organ and affection
C) They are not mentioned in revelation
D) They are impossible in the seen world

Key Takeaway
Human experiences do not limit divine attributes; our hearing and sight involve affection, but Allah's are unlike ours.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes, do you ever catch yourself imagining them as if they were like yours, just bigger or more powerful? What does this passage teach you about the danger of that assumption, and how will you actively remind yourself of the difference starting today?
?
The refutation argues that just because something is true in the created world does not make it true for Allah. In what areas of your faith (e.g., understanding of His mercy, justice, or knowledge) might you be unconsciously limiting Allah by your own experience? What will you do this week to correct that?
?
The objection assumes affection implies imperfection. How often do you measure divine actions by human standards of 'perfection'? Can you recall a moment when you objected to something in your life because it didn't fit your idea of how God should act? Based on this lesson, how would you now respond to that objection within yourself?

The Persistence of Attributes Without Objects
Al-Baqi's second objection and its refutation via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqi's second objection is refuted by showing that attributes can exist without active relation to an object.

Core Idea
Attributes exist even without objects — just like our senses.

Al-Baqi's second objection claims that affirming eternal hearing and sight for Allah is irrational because in pre-eternity there are no objects to be heard or seen. The answer demonstrates that the absence of a related object does not negate the attribute itself. This lesson unpacks that argument using the analogy of human senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read al-Baqi's objection carefully: why does he think eternal hearing and sight are irrational?
The objection relies on the assumption that an attribute must have an object at all times.
2
Understand the answer's core
The answer distinguishes between the attribute and its relation (ta'alluq). The attribute remains even when the relation is absent.
This is a key principle in kalam: attributes are not dependent on actualization.
3
See the analogy
Compare to human hearing and sight: they exist even when not currently perceiving anything. This shows that the objection is invalid.
The analogy refutes al-Baqi's claim that the absence of objects entails absence of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني : إثبات السمع والبصر في الأزل ولا مسموع ولا مبصر فيه ( خروج عن المعقول . والجواب أن انتفاء التعلق في الأزل لا يستلزم انتفاء الصفة فيه كما في سمعنا وبصرنا فإن خلوهما عن الإدراك بالفعل في وقت لا يوجب انتفاءهما أصلاً في ذلك الوقت .
The second [argument]: Affirming hearing and sight in pre-eternity when there is no heard or seen in it is contrary to reason. The answer: That the absence of relation in pre-eternity does not necessitate the absence of the attribute in it, just as in our hearing and sight: their being devoid of actual perception at a time does not entail their non-existence at that time.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents al-Baqi's second objection: that if hearing and sight are eternal attributes, they would have no object in pre-eternity, which is irrational. The answer demonstrates that the absence of a related object does not negate the attribute itself, analogously to how human hearing and sight persist even when not actually perceiving. Thus, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الأزل
التعلق
الإدراك بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An attribute must have an actual object at all times to exist.
Correction: An attribute can exist without actively relating to an object, as seen in human senses.

Checkpoint
How does the answer refute al-Baqi's second objection?
A) By denying that hearing and sight are attributes
B) By comparing to human senses that exist without actual perception
C) By saying that Allah creates objects in pre-eternity
D) By claiming that pre-eternity has its own objects

Key Takeaway
Eternal hearing and sight do not require eternal objects; the attributes persist without active relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have faculties of hearing and sight that exist even when you are not using them. But what about your spiritual faculties—your faith, your awareness of God? Do you only think of yourself as a believer when you are actively praying or remembering Him? Or is your faith a permanent attribute that persists even when you are distracted? If the latter, then why do you treat your faith as if it disappears when you sin or forget? Will you begin to see your faith as a constant reality that must be nurtured, not just activated in moments of worship?
?
God hears and sees eternally, even when there is nothing to hear or see. That means He is always aware of you, always present, even when you feel invisible or unheard. How does this truth confront the moments when you feel alone or unobserved? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that God's sight and hearing are fixed attributes that never cease—even when you are not performing religious acts? Will you let this belief transform your private moments, knowing you are always seen?
?
The objection al-Baqi raised is that it is irrational to affirm attributes without objects. But the answer shows that the attribute is more fundamental than its object. Reflect: Do you reduce your own worth or the worth of others to what you produce or achieve? Do you feel valuable only when you are 'doing something'? This lesson reminds you that existence and attributes are real even without external action. What would it look like for you to value yourself and others simply for who you are, not for what you do? Are you willing to extend that same grace to God's attributes—accepting His perfection even without immediate manifestation?

The Universality of Divine Hearing and Sight and the Dispute over Other Perceptions
Explains the scope of divine perceptions and the role of revelation vs. analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine hearing and sight are universally affirmed, why other perceptions like smell are disputed, and how the view on the nature of hearing affects the use of analogy.

Core Idea
Revelation establishes hearing and sight; analogy for other perceptions is contested.

This passage from a classical kalam commentary tackles a nuanced debate: which perceptions does Allah possess? While hearing and sight are affirmed by all Muslims based on revelation, the status of smell, taste, and touch is controversial. The author distinguishes between two views on hearing—whether it is identical to knowledge or an additional attribute—and shows how this distinction impacts the possibility of extending other perceptions via analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on philosophers and hearing/sight
Notice how the author explains that all prophets, including earlier ones, affirmed divine hearing and sight, so the philosophers of Islam are those who adhere to prophetic teachings.
2
Examine the argument against describing Allah with smell/taste/touch
Understand that the primary reason is lack of revelation, but then an analogy is proposed based on the 'second disclosure'—if it applies to heard/seen, it should apply to smelled etc.
The analogy is challenged by the distinction between hearing as knowledge vs. additional attribute.
3
Study Imam al-Haramayn's view
He argues that perception of tastes, smells, heat, and cold is obligatory to attribute to Allah due to the common proof, but one must not use verbs like 'smeller' because they imply physicality.
4
Compare the two underlying views on hearing
See that the analogy works if hearing is the same as knowledge, but fails if hearing is an additional attribute, because then the cause for affirming the second disclosure becomes revelation, not necessity.
This distinction is crucial for the entire debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإن وصفه تعالى إلخ) وأيضاً قوله : فقالت الفلاسفة إلخ تفصيل لاختلاف المسلمين كما يدل عليه صريح كلام المحصل ثم الظاهر أن المراد بفلاسفة الإسلام، الفلاسفة المتمسكون بأقوال الأنبياء مطلقاً لأن الأنبياء المتقدمين أيضاً قالوا بثبوت السمع والبصر. قوله : ( وإنما لم يوصف بالشم إلخ) قيل : لا خلاف في عدم جواز وصفه تعالى بهذه الثلاثة لعدم ورود النقل لكن إذا ثبت التغاير بين الانكشافين في المسموع والمبصر ثبت التغاير بينهما في المشموم وأمثاله، وإذا ثبت الانكشاف الثاني في المسموع والمبصر له تعالى وجب إثباته في المشموم وأمثاله أيضاً. لئلا يلزم التجهيل تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. ولذا قال إمام الحرمين: يجب وصفه تعالى بإدراك الطعوم والروائح والحرارة والبرودة كما يجب وصفه تعالى بإدراك المسموع والمبصر للشركة في الدليل لكن لا يقال : إنه تعالى شام ذائق لامس لأنها تنبئ عن الاتصالات الجسمانية. وأنت خبير بأن هذا إنما يتأتى على القول بأن السمع مثلاً نفس العلم بالمسموع، وأما على القول بأنه صفة زائدة ،فلا لأن قياس إثبات الانكشاف الثاني في المشموم وأمثاله له تعالى على إثباته له في المسموع والمبصر مشكل إذ ليس العلة في هذا الإثبات، حينئذ لزوم التجهيز بنفيه. كيف وهذا الانكشاف عند القائل بأن كلاً من السمع والبصر صفة زائدة على الصفات الخمس الباقية ليس علماً حتى يلزم التجهيل من نفيه على أن قابلية الذات للاتصاف بهذه الانكشافات شرط في لزوم التجهيل على تقدير انتفائه ودون إثباتها خرط القتادة . فالعلة فيه ورود النقل المفقود في المشموم وأمثاله . قوله : خروج عن المعقول قالوا ولا ينتقض ذلك بالعالمية والقادرية الأزليتين لأن يصح تعلقها بالمعدوم وبما سيوجد والقادرية يصح تعلقها بأحداث الفعل في الوقت الثاني بخلاف السامعية والمبصرية. العالمية
قوله : ( فإن خلوهما عن الإدراك بالفعل قيل: تجويز خلو الباري عن الإدراك السمعي والبصري مع بقاء منشئهما أعني السمع والبصر مذهب أبي الحسين بعينه وقد أبطلوه. فكيف يلتزمون هذا وجوابه ما أشرنا إليه آنفا من أن الإدراك السمعي والبصري عند من يثبت السمع والبصر صفتين زائدتين أمر وراء العلم فلا يلزم من انتفائهما بالفعل التجهيل كما لزم أبا الحسين على زعمهم فليتأمل
His saying: 'For His description, exalted, etc.' And also his saying: 'For the philosophers said, etc.' is a detailed account of the disagreement among Muslims, as indicated by the explicit words of al-Muhassal. Then it appears that by 'the philosophers of Islam' is meant the philosophers who adhere to the sayings of the prophets absolutely, because the earlier prophets also affirmed hearing and sight. His saying: 'And He is not described with smell, etc.' It is said: There is no disagreement about the impermissibility of describing Him, exalted, with these three due to lack of revelation, but if the distinction between the two disclosures in the heard and seen is established, then the distinction between them in the smelled and its likes is established. And if the second disclosure in the heard and seen is established for Him, exalted, then it is necessary to affirm it in the smelled and its likes as well, lest ignorance be attributed to Him — exalted is He above that greatly. For this reason, Imam al-Haramayn said: It is obligatory to describe Him, exalted, with perception of tastes, smells, heat, and cold, just as it is obligatory to describe Him with perception of the heard and seen due to the commonality in the proof, but it is not said that He is a smeller, taster, toucher, because these indicate physical attachments. And you are aware that this is only feasible on the view that hearing, for example, is the same as knowledge of the heard. But on the view that it is an additional attribute, it is not, because analogizing the affirmation of the second disclosure in the smelled and its likes for Him to its affirmation for Him in the heard and seen is problematic, since the cause for this affirmation is not then the necessity of ignorance by its negation. How so? And this disclosure, according to the one who holds that each of hearing and sight is an additional attribute beyond the remaining five attributes, is not knowledge such that ignorance would follow from its negation, given that the capacity of the essence to be qualified with these disclosures is a condition for the necessity of ignorance assuming its absence, and establishing that is as difficult as cutting a thorn tree. So the cause for it is the revelation that is lacking for smell and its likes. His saying: 'contrary to reason.' They said: And this is not refuted by the eternal will and power, because their relation to non-existent and future existents is valid, and power's relation to the events of the act in the second time is valid, unlike hearing and seeing. 'al-alamiyya'
His saying: 'For their being devoid of actual perception.' It is said: Permitting the absence of auditory and visual perception in the Creator while their sources, i.e., hearing and sight, remain is precisely the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn, and they have refuted it. So how can they adopt this? And its answer is what we pointed to earlier: that auditory and visual perception, according to those who affirm hearing and sight as two additional attributes, is something beyond knowledge, so their absence in actuality does not necessitate ignorance, as it did for Abu al-Husayn, in their view. So reflect.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy commentary on the sixth section, addressing several points: the classification of philosophers, the disagreement about whether Allah should be described with additional perceptions like smell, taste, and touch based on analogy, the view of Imam al-Haramayn, and the distinction between the views that hearing is identical to knowledge versus an additional attribute. It also responds to an objection about the absence of actual perception, defending that the persistence of the attribute without actual perception does not entail ignorance.

Key Terms
القائل بصفتين زائدتين
إمام الحرمين
الانكشاف الثاني
المحصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine hearing and sight are contested among Muslims, but the text confirms they are universally affirmed among those following the prophets.
Correction: The passage shows that hearing and sight are affirmed by all, including the philosophers who follow prophets.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not said that Allah is a 'smeller'?
A) Because revelation for that is lacking
B) Because it implies physical organs
C) Because it is an additional attribute
D) Because analogy fails

Key Takeaway
The affirmation of divine hearing and sight is universal, but other perceptions require revelation or carefully conditioned analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
