Objection: If Speech Is One, Why Not All Attributes?
An objection to the unity of speech and the Ash'ari response based on distinct effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological objection that if divine speech is one due to relations, then all attributes could be reduced to one meaning or even to the essence, and the Ash'ari response based on distinct effects of power and will.

Core Idea
If speech can be one despite multiple expressions, why not all attributes? Only distinct effects require distinct attributes.

This passage presents a critical note (tanbih) that challenges the commonly held Ash'ari thesis that divine speech is one, with its diversity arising from external relations. The objection pushes further: if that is acceptable, then why cannot all attributes be reduced to a single meaning, or even to the divine essence itself? The response from the Ash'ari school is that attributes like power and will have distinct effects on creation, necessitating their real distinction, unlike speech which has no effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of objection and response. Pay attention to the logical structure.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two levels of the objection
First, that all attributes could be one meaning; second, that they could all reduce to the essence. Notice how each step extends the initial claim about speech.
3
Examine the Ash'ari response
The response distinguishes power (creates existence) and will (specifies), arguing that differing effects require distinct agents. Contrast with speech which has no effect.
4
Reflect on the significance
Understand why this distinction matters for defending the multiplicity of divine attributes against reductionism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : كلامه واحد إلخ فيه بحث لأن الكلام إذا كان أمراً واحداً وكان اختلاف العبارات عنها بسبب التعلقات الخارجية فلم لا يجوز أن يكون العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات راجعة إلى معنى واحد فيكون اختلاف التعبيرات عنها بسبب التعلقات لا اختلافه بسبب ذاته، وذلك بأن يسمى إرادة عند تعلقه بالتخصيص وقدرة عند تعلقه بالإيجاد، وهكذا سائر الصفات وإن جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يعود ذلك كله إلى نفس الذات من غير احتياج الصفات أجاب الأصحاب بأن القدرة معنى من شأنه يأتي الإيجاد به والإرادة معنى من شأنه تخصيص الحادث بحالة دون حالة وعند اختلاف التأثيرات لا بد من الاختلاف في نفس المؤثر وهذا بخلاف الكلام فإن تعلقاته لا توجب أثراً فضلاً عن كونه مختلفاً وفيه نظر إذ امتناع صدور الآثار من المؤثر الواحد أصل الفلاسفة لا المتكلمين ولو سلم فهو موجب للاختلاف في نفس القدرة لأن القدرة مؤثرة في الوجود، والوجود عند الشيخ الأشعري ومتابعيه في ذلك نفس الذات كما مر ولما كانت الذوات مختلفة لزم أن يكون تأثير القدرة في آثار مختلفة فيلزم أن تكون مختلفة وليس كذلك، وأيضاً ما ذكره وإن تمشى لهم في القدرة والإرادة لم يتمش في باقي الصفات كالعلم والحياة والسمع والبصر لعدم كونها مؤثرة في أثر ما قال الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار والحق أن ما أورد من الإشكال على القول باتحاد الكلام وعود الاختلاف إلى التعلقات المختلفة.
A note: 'His speech is one, etc.' – there is discussion in it, because if speech is one thing and the difference of expressions concerning it is due to external relations, then why is it not permissible that knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes go back to one meaning, so that the difference of expressions concerning them is due to relations, not due to difference in their essence? That is, it would be called 'will' when it relates to specification, and 'power' when it relates to creation, and so on for the rest of the attributes. And if that is allowed, then why is it not allowed that all of that goes back to the essence itself, without the need for attributes? The companions answered: Power is a meaning the import of which is to bring about existence, and will is a meaning the import of which is to specify the temporal event in one state rather than another, and when effects differ, there must be difference in the very agent. This is unlike speech, for its relations do not necessitate an effect, let alone its being different. But there is consideration: because the impossibility of multiple effects from one agent is a principle of the philosophers, not of the theologians. And if it is granted, it would necessitate difference in power itself, because power affects existence, and existence according to al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari and his followers is the essence itself, as has passed. And since essences differ, the effect of power must be in different effects, which would necessitate that the powers are different, but that is not the case. Also, what he mentioned, even if it applies to power and will, does not apply to the remaining attributes such as knowledge, life, hearing, and sight, because they do not effect an effect. Al-Amidi said in Abkar al-Afkar: The truth is that the objection raised against the thesis of the unity of speech and the returning of difference to different relations...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a critical note (tanbih) discussing the thesis that all divine attributes reduce to a single meaning, similar to the claim that speech is one. It begins by considering: if speech is one and its various expressions are due to external relations, why cannot the same be said for other attributes like power and will? And even further, why cannot all attributes be reduced to the essence itself? The Ash'ari response is that power and will have distinct effects—power creates existence, will specifies—so they must be distinct. Speech, however, does not produce an effect, so it can be one. The author then raises a counter: the principle that one agent cannot produce multiple effects is a philosophical one, not accepted by theologians. Even if it were, it would imply that power, affecting existence, must be multiple because existents are multiple. Moreover, the argument does not hold for attributes like knowledge, which do not produce effects. The passage concludes by citing al-Amidi's view that the truth is that the objection to the unity of speech and the difference due to relations is problematic.

التعلقات
القدرة
الإرادة
المؤثر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If divine speech can be one despite different expressions, then all other attributes must also be one.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that attributes like power and will produce different effects in creation, so they must be truly distinct. Speech does not produce effects, so it can be one.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why cannot all attributes be reduced to one meaning like speech?
A) Because speech is an exception
B) Because attributes like power and will have distinct effects
C) Because the Quran says so
D) Because the attributes are all different in essence

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school defends the multiplicity of attributes by arguing that power and will have distinct causal effects, while speech does not, so only the latter can be considered unitary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Ash'ari response insists that we cannot reduce God's attributes to His essence without losing their distinct functions. Have you ever, in your own worship or understanding, treated God's attributes as mere names rather than real? What would it mean for you to take them more seriously?

Critique of the Ash'ari Response: Problems with Distinct Effects
A critique of the Ash'ari response, raising three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological critique of the Ash'ari response, including the philosophical nature of the 'one agent, one effect' principle, the problem of power's effects, and the failure to account for attributes without effects.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari response relies on a philosophical principle and fails for non-effective attributes like knowledge.

This passage continues the critical note by questioning the Ash'ari response just presented. It raises three major problems: first, the principle that one agent cannot produce multiple effects is a philosophical position, not a theological one. Second, even if accepted, it would imply that power itself must be multiple because it affects essences which are multiple. Third, the argument does not apply to attributes like knowledge, life, hearing, and sight, which have no direct effect. The passage ends by citing al-Amidi's affirmation that the objection to the unity of speech remains problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Understand the flow of critique: first the philosophical principle, then its implication for power, then the failure for other attributes.
2
Identify the three critiques
List them: (1) philosophical principle, (2) implication for power, (3) other attributes. Note how each builds on the previous.
3
Reflect on the significance of citing al-Amidi
Consider why the author ends with al-Amidi's statement that the truth is the objection remains. This shows the issue is unsettled.
4
Connect to wider theology
Think about how this debate relates to the nature of divine attributes and the limits of analogical reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : كلامه واحد إلخ فيه بحث لأن الكلام إذا كان أمراً واحداً وكان اختلاف العبارات عنها بسبب التعلقات الخارجية فلم لا يجوز أن يكون العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات راجعة إلى معنى واحد فيكون اختلاف التعبيرات عنها بسبب التعلقات لا اختلافه بسبب ذاته، وذلك بأن يسمى إرادة عند تعلقه بالتخصيص وقدرة عند تعلقه بالإيجاد، وهكذا سائر الصفات وإن جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يعود ذلك كله إلى نفس الذات من غير احتياج الصفات أجاب الأصحاب بأن القدرة معنى من شأنه يأتي الإيجاد به والإرادة معنى من شأنه تخصيص الحادث بحالة دون حالة وعند اختلاف التأثيرات لا بد من الاختلاف في نفس المؤثر وهذا بخلاف الكلام فإن تعلقاته لا توجب أثراً فضلاً عن كونه مختلفاً وفيه نظر إذ امتناع صدور الآثار من المؤثر الواحد أصل الفلاسفة لا المتكلمين ولو سلم فهو موجب للاختلاف في نفس القدرة لأن القدرة مؤثرة في الوجود، والوجود عند الشيخ الأشعري ومتابعيه في ذلك نفس الذات كما مر ولما كانت الذوات مختلفة لزم أن يكون تأثير القدرة في آثار مختلفة فيلزم أن تكون مختلفة وليس كذلك، وأيضاً ما ذكره وإن تمشى لهم في القدرة والإرادة لم يتمش في باقي الصفات كالعلم والحياة والسمع والبصر لعدم كونها مؤثرة في أثر ما قال الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار والحق أن ما أورد من الإشكال على القول باتحاد الكلام وعود الاختلاف إلى التعلقات المختلفة.
A note: 'His speech is one, etc.' – there is discussion in it, because if speech is one thing and the difference of expressions concerning it is due to external relations, then why is it not permissible that knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes go back to one meaning, so that the difference of expressions concerning them is due to relations, not due to difference in their essence? That is, it would be called 'will' when it relates to specification, and 'power' when it relates to creation, and so on for the rest of the attributes. And if that is allowed, then why is it not allowed that all of that goes back to the essence itself, without the need for attributes? The companions answered: Power is a meaning the import of which is to bring about existence, and will is a meaning the import of which is to specify the temporal event in one state rather than another, and when effects differ, there must be difference in the very agent. This is unlike speech, for its relations do not necessitate an effect, let alone its being different. But there is consideration: because the impossibility of multiple effects from one agent is a principle of the philosophers, not of the theologians. And if it is granted, it would necessitate difference in power itself, because power affects existence, and existence according to al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari and his followers is the essence itself, as has passed. And since essences differ, the effect of power must be in different effects, which would necessitate that the powers are different, but that is not the case. Also, what he mentioned, even if it applies to power and will, does not apply to the remaining attributes such as knowledge, life, hearing, and sight, because they do not effect an effect. Al-Amidi said in Abkar al-Afkar: The truth is that the objection raised against the thesis of the unity of speech and the returning of difference to different relations...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a critical note (tanbih) discussing the thesis that all divine attributes reduce to a single meaning, similar to the claim that speech is one. It begins by considering: if speech is one and its various expressions are due to external relations, why cannot the same be said for other attributes like power and will? And even further, why cannot all attributes be reduced to the essence itself? The Ash'ari response is that power and will have distinct effects—power creates existence, will specifies—so they must be distinct. Speech, however, does not produce an effect, so it can be one. The author then raises a counter: the principle that one agent cannot produce multiple effects is a philosophical one, not accepted by theologians. Even if it were, it would imply that power, affecting existence, must be multiple because existents are multiple. Moreover, the argument does not hold for attributes like knowledge, which do not produce effects. The passage concludes by citing al-Amidi's view that the truth is that the objection to the unity of speech and the difference due to relations is problematic.

Key Terms
المؤثر
الفلاسفة
العلم
الحياة
السمع
البصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari response successfully defends the distinctness of all attributes.
Correction: The critique shows the response relies on a philosophical principle, fails for power itself, and does not apply to attributes like knowledge, life, hearing, and sight.

Checkpoint
What is one of the critiques raised against the Ash'ari response?
A) That speech is not actually one
B) That the principle of one agent-one effect is a philosophical principle
C) That power does not affect existence
D) That all attributes have effects

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to attribute reductionism is itself vulnerable to critique: it relies on a philosophical principle, fails for non-causal attributes, and may lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument that seems neat and tidy, do you stop to check whether its foundational principles are truly from revelation or merely borrowed from human philosophy? How can you cultivate a critical eye without falling into arrogance?
?
This passage shows that even great scholars like al-Ash'ari can make imperfect arguments. Does your own understanding of God's attributes allow for correction? Are you willing to abandon a comfortable explanation if it does not align with sound theology?
?
The critique here focuses on attributes like knowledge and life that do not have visible effects. Do you value those attributes in your own life? Do you strive to know God and live in His presence, or are you only concerned with what you can 'effect' in the world?

The Ninth and Tenth Arguments and Their Response
Al-Iji presents the ninth and tenth arguments from opponents and gives a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two specific arguments (past tense reports and abrogation) that opponents use to claim the Quran is created, and why the response shows they only prove the createdness of the uttered words, not the eternal speech of Allah.

Core Idea
Past tense and abrogation arguments miss the mark—they only prove the Quran's uttered words are created.

Al-Iji presents two more arguments from the ten that opponents use to argue for the createdness of the Quran. The ninth focuses on past-tense reports, and the tenth on abrogation. He then gives a unified answer that these arguments only apply to the literal expression, not the eternal attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: two numbered arguments followed by a response.
The response is brief but crucial for the whole debate.
2
Analyze the ninth argument
Identify why the opponent claims that past-tense verbs like 'We sent it down' imply temporality. Understand the reasoning: if speech is eternal, then reporting a past event relative to eternity would be false.
3
Analyze the tenth argument
Understand the abrogation argument: abrogation removes or ends a ruling, but what is eternal cannot cease. So the Quran must be created.
Abrogation (naskh) is widely accepted in Islam.
4
Understand the unified response
Notice that al-Iji answers all ten arguments together: they only prove that the uttered expression (al-lafẓ) is created, not the eternal speech (al-kalām al-qadīm). This is the key distinction.
The dispute is about the eternal speech, not the physical words.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this response is effective. It concedes that the Quranic text is temporal in some sense, but preserves the eternal attribute of speech.
This aligns with the Ash'ari position on the Quran.

التاسع : أنه تعالى أخبر بلفظ الماضي نحو إنا أنزلناه ، إنا أرسلنا ) ولا شك أنه لا نزال ولا إرسال في الأزل، فلو كان كلامه قديماً لكان كذباً لأنه إخبار بالوقوع في ما الماضي ولا يتصور هو ماض بالقياس إلى الأزل . ( العاشر : النسخ حق بإجماع الأمة ووقع في القرآن وهو ( رفع ) أو انتهاء ( و ) لا شيء منهما يتصور في القديم لأن ( ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه والإمام الرازي جعل هذين الوجهين في الأربعين من الأدلة المعقولة، والحق ما اختاره المصنف (والجواب : ( عن الوجوه العشرة أنها تدل على حدوث اللفظ كما لا يخفى على المتأمل ( وهو غير المتنازع فيه كما تحققته
The ninth: That He, exalted, informed using past tense like 'Indeed We sent it down', 'Indeed We sent'. There is no doubt that there is no sending down and no sending in pre-eternity. If His speech were eternal, it would be a lie because it is an informing of occurrence in the past, and it is inconceivable that it is past relative to pre-eternity. The tenth: Abrogation is true by consensus of the community and occurs in the Qur'an, and it is a removal or termination, and neither of them is conceivable in the eternal because what is proven eternal is impossible to cease. Imam al-Razi placed these two aspects among the forty rational proofs. The truth is what the author chose. And the answer: (Concerning the ten aspects, they indicate the temporality of the utterance, as is not hidden from the contemplator, and it is not what is disputed, as you have realized.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents two of the ten arguments (wujūh) used by opponents to prove that the Quran is created (ḥādith). The ninth argument: Allah uses past-tense verbs like 'We sent it down' and 'We sent'. Since sending down and sending are temporal acts, they cannot be eternal; if God's speech were eternal, such statements would be false because they report an event in the past relative to eternity. The tenth argument: Abrogation (naskh) is agreed upon and occurs in the Quran, meaning a ruling is lifted or ended. But in the eternal, nothing can be removed or ended because what is eternal cannot cease. The author (al-Iji) sides with Imam al-Razi in considering these two as rational proofs. The answer (al-jawāb) is given collectively for all ten arguments: they only prove that the uttered expression (al-lafẓ) is created, which is not disputed. The real point of contention is the eternal speech (al-kalām al-qadīm) which remains unchanged.

Key Terms
كلام قديم (speech eternal)
حدوث اللفظ (temporality of the utterance)
نسخ (abrogation)
إخبار بالماضي (past tense report)
الوجوه العشرة (the ten aspects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ninth and tenth arguments prove that the eternal speech of Allah is created.
Correction: The response clarifies that these arguments only prove the createdness of the uttered words (al-lafẓ), not the eternal attribute of speech (al-kalām al-qadīm), which remains eternal and unchanged.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer (al-jawāb) to the ten arguments?
A) They prove the Quran is created in its essence.
B) They only prove the createdness of the literal word (al-lafẓ), not the eternal speech.
C) They are invalid because abrogation does not occur.
D) They are only valid for the past tense argument.

Key Takeaway
The ninth and tenth arguments only prove the createdness of the uttered words, not the eternal speech of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you consciously separate the created letters and sounds from the eternal meaning they carry? What would change in your reverence if you forgot this distinction?
?
The opponents argued that past tense implies temporality, so the Quran must be created. Yet the response says their argument only proves the utterance is created. Does this distinction feel like a clever escape to you, or does it deepen your understanding of Allah's transcendence? Be honest about your initial reaction.
?
Abrogation means some divine commands are temporary. Does that make you question the constancy of Allah's will, or does it strengthen your trust in His wisdom that adapts to times? How does this lesson's response affect your confidence in the eternal nature of His speech despite the change of rulings?

Objection to Unity of Speech and Ash'ari Response
Al-Iji presents an objection to the unity of divine speech and the Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if speech is one by relation, why not all attributes, and the Ash'ari response based on effects.

Core Idea
If speech's unity is saved by relations, why not reduce all attributes to one?

Al-Iji presents a sharp objection against the Ash'ari claim that divine speech is one and its types are merely relational. He asks why knowledge, power, and other attributes cannot also be unified under a single meaning, with differences only in relation. The Ash'ari response and its limitations are then examined.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is raised: if speech is one and differences are due to external relations, why not apply this to all attributes?
Notice the analogical argument: if speech's multiplicity is only relational, the same could be said for power and will.
2
Understand the Ash'ari response
The companions answer that power and will produce different effects, requiring internal difference, while speech's relations produce no effect.
This response attempts to distinguish speech from effective attributes.
3
Analyze the critique of the response
Al-Iji critiques the response: (1) the principle that one agent cannot produce multiple effects is philosophical, not theological; (2) even if accepted, it would force difference in power itself; (3) it fails for non-effective attributes like knowledge and life.
Pay attention to the logical structure of the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : كلامه واحد إلخ فيه بحث لأن الكلام إذا كان أمراً واحداً وكان اختلاف العبارات عنها بسبب التعلقات الخارجية فلم لا يجوز أن يكون العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات راجعة إلى معنى واحد فيكون اختلاف التعبيرات عنها بسبب التعلقات لا اختلافه بسبب ذاته، وذلك بأن يسمى إرادة عند تعلقه بالتخصيص وقدرة عند تعلقه بالإيجاد، وهكذا سائر الصفات وإن جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يعود ذلك كله إلى نفس الذات من غير احتياج الصفات أجاب الأصحاب بأن القدرة معنى من شأنه يأتي الإيجاد به والإرادة معنى من شأنه تخصيص الحادث بحالة دون حالة وعند اختلاف التأثيرات لا بد من الاختلاف في نفس المؤثر وهذا بخلاف الكلام فإن تعلقاته لا توجب أثراً فضلاً عن كونه مختلفاً وفيه نظر إذ امتناع صدور الآثار من المؤثر الواحد أصل الفلاسفة لا المتكلمين ولو سلم فهو موجب للاختلاف في نفس القدرة لأن القدرة مؤثرة في الوجود، والوجود عند الشيخ الأشعري ومتابعيه في ذلك نفس الذات كما مر ولما كانت الذوات مختلفة لزم أن يكون تأثير القدرة في آثار مختلفة فيلزم أن تكون مختلفة وليس كذلك، وأيضاً ما ذكره وإن تمشى لهم في القدرة والإرادة لم يتمش في باقي الصفات كالعلم والحياة والسمع والبصر لعدم كونها مؤثرة في أثر ما قال الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار والحق أن ما أورد من الإشكال على القول باتحاد الكلام وعود الاختلاف إلى التعلقات المختلفة المرصد الرابع - المقصد السابع : في أنه تعالى متكلم تنبیه کلامه تعالى ( واحد عندنا لما مر في القدرة من أنها لو تعددت لاستندت إلى الذات إما بالاختيار أو بالإيجاب وهما باطلان أما الأول فلأن القديم لا يستند إلى المختار، وأما الثاني فلأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع الأعداد سواء فيلزم وجود قدر لا يتناهى (و) أما ( انقسامه إلى الأمر والنهي والاستفهام والخبر والنداء ) فإنما هو ( بحسب التعلق فذلك الكلام الواحد باعتبار تعلقه بشيء على وجه مخصوص يكون خبراً وباعتبار تعلقه بشيء آخر أو على وجه آخر يكون أمراً، وكذا الحال في البواقي ( وقيل : ( كلامه ( خمسة) هي الأقسام المذكورة، ( وقال ابن سعيد من الأشاعرة هو في الأزل واحد وليس متصفا بشيء من تلك الخمسة وإنما يصير أحدهما فيما لا يزال وأورد عليه أنها أنواعه فلا يوجد (دونها ) إذ الجنس لا يوجد إلا في ضمن شيء من أنواعه ( والجواب : منع ذلك في أنواع تحصل بحسب التعلق ) يعني أنها ليست أنواعاً حقيقية له حتى يلزم ما ذكرتم، بل هي أنواع اعتبارية تحصل له بحسب تعلقه بالأشياء فجاز أن يوجد جنسها بدونها ومعها أيضاً، فليس كلام ابن سعيد ببعيد جداً
Caution: His speech is one etc. There is investigation in it, because if speech is one thing and the difference of expressions about it is due to external relations, then why is it not permissible that knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes return to one meaning, so that the difference of expressions about them is due to relations, not due to difference in essence? For example, it is called will when it relates to specification, power when it relates to bringing into existence, and so on for the rest of attributes. And if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible that all of that returns to the essence itself without needing attributes? The companions answered: Power is a meaning whose property is to bring about existence by it, and will is a meaning whose property is to specify the contingent with a state rather than another, and when effects differ, there must be difference in the affecting agent itself. This is unlike speech, because its relations do not produce an effect, let alone it being different. And there is consideration in it, because the impossibility of effects emanating from one agent is a principle of the philosophers, not of the theologians. And even if granted, it necessitates difference in power itself, because power affects existence, and existence according to al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī and his followers in that is the essence itself, as has passed, and since essences are different, it would be necessary that the effect of power be in different effects, so it would be necessary that they be different, but it is not so. Also, what he mentioned, even if it works for them regarding power and will, does not work for the remaining attributes like knowledge, life, hearing, and sight, because they do not affect any effect. Al-Amidi said in Abkār al-Afkār: The truth is that what was brought as an objection against the statement of the unity of speech and that difference returns to different relations is problematic. The Fourth Observatory - The Seventh Objective: That He, exalted, is a speaker. Caution: His speech, exalted, is one according to us, because of what has passed in [the discussion of] power, that if it were multiple, it would be dependent on the essence either by choice or by necessitation, and both are invalid. As for the first, because the eternal is not dependent on the chosen. As for the second, because the relation of the necessitator to all individuals is equal, so it would necessitate the existence of infinite power. And as for its division into command, prohibition, interrogation, information, and call, it is only according to relation. So that one speech, by virtue of its relation to something in a specific manner, is information, and by virtue of its relation to something else or in another manner, is command, and likewise for the rest. And it was said: His speech is five, the mentioned divisions. And Ibn Saʿīd of the Ashʿarīs said: It is in pre-eternity one and not qualified by any of those five, but it becomes one of them in the future. And it was objected against him: They are its types, so it does not exist without them, because a genus does not exist except within one of its types. And the answer: Denial of that for types that arise according to relation, meaning that they are not real types of it such that what you mentioned follows; rather, they are relational types that arise for it according to its relation to things, so it is permissible that its genus exist without them and with them. So the statement of Ibn Saʿīd is not very remote.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion (tanbīh) defending the unity of divine speech (kalām) and explaining how it can be described as command, prohibition, etc. without multiplicity. The author first presents an objection: if speech is one and differences are due to relations, why can't all attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) be reduced to one meaning, or even to the essence itself? The companions (aṣḥāb) respond that power and will have distinct effects (bringing into existence vs. specification), so the affecting agent must differ, whereas speech's relations produce no effect. The author notes this response has problems: the principle that effects cannot come from one agent is philosophical, not theological. Even if accepted, power would then need to vary with its effects, but the Ashʿarī view that existence is the essence itself leads to contradiction. Also, the response fails for non-effective attributes like knowledge and life. Then al-Āmidī's view is cited that the objection against unity of speech is difficult. The text then repeats the header and gives the standard Ashʿarī position: divine speech is one, proven by the argument from power (if multiple, they would either be chosen or necessitated, both impossible). Its division into five types (command, prohibition, interrogation, information, call) is only by relation (bi-ḥasab al-taʿalluq). One speech becomes information by relating to something in a certain manner, command by relating differently, etc. Then a different opinion (qīla) says speech is five distinct types. Ibn Saʿīd says it is one in pre-eternity and only becomes one of those in the future. The objection is that a genus cannot exist without its types. The answer denies that these are real types; they are relational (iʿtibāriyya), so the genus can exist without them. Thus Ibn Saʿīd's view is not far-fetched.

Key Terms
كلام واحد
تعلق
إرادة
قدرة
صِفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the unity of speech by relation implies all attributes are essentially one, or that the Ash'ari response is conclusive.
Correction: The Ash'ari response is critiqued as weak; the passage shows it relies on assumptions not universally accepted.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response in this passage, why can speech be one while power and will cannot?
A) Speech is an attribute of action, not of essence.
B) Power and will produce distinct effects, while speech's relations produce no effect.
C) Speech is temporally created.
D) Power and will are identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the objectioin that all attributes could be unified is based on the distinction between effective and non-effective relations, but this response faces serious criticisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical objection like al-Iji's to a position you previously accepted, do you honestly engage with it or quickly dismiss it? What does your response reveal about your commitment to truth over comfort?
?
The Ashʿari response relies on a distinction between effective and non-effective relations. Can you identify an area in your own beliefs where you rely on a distinction that may not hold up under scrutiny? Are you willing to test it?
?
If you were to realize that a theological proof you used to support a core belief is flawed, how would your faith survive that? What would you need to rebuild?

Unity of Speech and Division by Relation
Al-Iji states the Ash'ari proof for unity of speech and its relational division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof for the unity of divine speech and how its division into command, prohibition, etc. is only by relation.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one; it becomes command or news only by relation to what is spoken about.

This passage presents the standard Ash'ari position: God's speech (kalām) is a single eternal attribute, proven by the same argument used for power. Its apparent multiplicity into command, prohibition, interrogation, information, and call is not real but relational. The lesson also examines the view of Ibn Sa'īd that speech is not qualified by these types in pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof for unity
Read how the text argues for the oneness of speech by analogizing to the proof for the oneness of power.
The proof is based on impossibility of dependence on a choosing agent or necessitation.
2
See the relational division
The passage explains how one speech becomes command, prohibition, etc. by different relations to things.
Note that this preserves unity while allowing for scriptural diversity.
3
Examine Ibn Sa'īd's view and response
Ibn Sa'īd says speech is one in pre-eternity without any of these types, becoming them later. The objection and answer clarify the notion of 'relational types' (anwāʿ iʿtibāriyya).
Consider how this view differs from saying speech is five types in pre-eternity.

تنبيه : كلامه واحد إلخ فيه بحث لأن الكلام إذا كان أمراً واحداً وكان اختلاف العبارات عنها بسبب التعلقات الخارجية فلم لا يجوز أن يكون العلم والقدرة وسائر الصفات راجعة إلى معنى واحد فيكون اختلاف التعبيرات عنها بسبب التعلقات لا اختلافه بسبب ذاته، وذلك بأن يسمى إرادة عند تعلقه بالتخصيص وقدرة عند تعلقه بالإيجاد، وهكذا سائر الصفات وإن جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يعود ذلك كله إلى نفس الذات من غير احتياج الصفات أجاب الأصحاب بأن القدرة معنى من شأنه يأتي الإيجاد به والإرادة معنى من شأنه تخصيص الحادث بحالة دون حالة وعند اختلاف التأثيرات لا بد من الاختلاف في نفس المؤثر وهذا بخلاف الكلام فإن تعلقاته لا توجب أثراً فضلاً عن كونه مختلفاً وفيه نظر إذ امتناع صدور الآثار من المؤثر الواحد أصل الفلاسفة لا المتكلمين ولو سلم فهو موجب للاختلاف في نفس القدرة لأن القدرة مؤثرة في الوجود، والوجود عند الشيخ الأشعري ومتابعيه في ذلك نفس الذات كما مر ولما كانت الذوات مختلفة لزم أن يكون تأثير القدرة في آثار مختلفة فيلزم أن تكون مختلفة وليس كذلك، وأيضاً ما ذكره وإن تمشى لهم في القدرة والإرادة لم يتمش في باقي الصفات كالعلم والحياة والسمع والبصر لعدم كونها مؤثرة في أثر ما قال الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار والحق أن ما أورد من الإشكال على القول باتحاد الكلام وعود الاختلاف إلى التعلقات المختلفة المرصد الرابع - المقصد السابع : في أنه تعالى متكلم تنبیه کلامه تعالى ( واحد عندنا لما مر في القدرة من أنها لو تعددت لاستندت إلى الذات إما بالاختيار أو بالإيجاب وهما باطلان أما الأول فلأن القديم لا يستند إلى المختار، وأما الثاني فلأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع الأعداد سواء فيلزم وجود قدر لا يتناهى (و) أما ( انقسامه إلى الأمر والنهي والاستفهام والخبر والنداء ) فإنما هو ( بحسب التعلق فذلك الكلام الواحد باعتبار تعلقه بشيء على وجه مخصوص يكون خبراً وباعتبار تعلقه بشيء آخر أو على وجه آخر يكون أمراً، وكذا الحال في البواقي ( وقيل : ( كلامه ( خمسة) هي الأقسام المذكورة، ( وقال ابن سعيد من الأشاعرة هو في الأزل واحد وليس متصفا بشيء من تلك الخمسة وإنما يصير أحدهما فيما لا يزال وأورد عليه أنها أنواعه فلا يوجد (دونها ) إذ الجنس لا يوجد إلا في ضمن شيء من أنواعه ( والجواب : منع ذلك في أنواع تحصل بحسب التعلق ) يعني أنها ليست أنواعاً حقيقية له حتى يلزم ما ذكرتم، بل هي أنواع اعتبارية تحصل له بحسب تعلقه بالأشياء فجاز أن يوجد جنسها بدونها ومعها أيضاً، فليس كلام ابن سعيد ببعيد جداً
Caution: His speech is one etc. There is investigation in it, because if speech is one thing and the difference of expressions about it is due to external relations, then why is it not permissible that knowledge, power, and the rest of the attributes return to one meaning, so that the difference of expressions about them is due to relations, not due to difference in essence? For example, it is called will when it relates to specification, power when it relates to bringing into existence, and so on for the rest of attributes. And if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible that all of that returns to the essence itself without needing attributes? The companions answered: Power is a meaning whose property is to bring about existence by it, and will is a meaning whose property is to specify the contingent with a state rather than another, and when effects differ, there must be difference in the affecting agent itself. This is unlike speech, because its relations do not produce an effect, let alone it being different. And there is consideration in it, because the impossibility of effects emanating from one agent is a principle of the philosophers, not of the theologians. And even if granted, it necessitates difference in power itself, because power affects existence, and existence according to al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī and his followers in that is the essence itself, as has passed, and since essences are different, it would be necessary that the effect of power be in different effects, so it would be necessary that they be different, but it is not so. Also, what he mentioned, even if it works for them regarding power and will, does not work for the remaining attributes like knowledge, life, hearing, and sight, because they do not affect any effect. Al-Amidi said in Abkār al-Afkār: The truth is that what was brought as an objection against the statement of the unity of speech and that difference returns to different relations is problematic. The Fourth Observatory - The Seventh Objective: That He, exalted, is a speaker. Caution: His speech, exalted, is one according to us, because of what has passed in [the discussion of] power, that if it were multiple, it would be dependent on the essence either by choice or by necessitation, and both are invalid. As for the first, because the eternal is not dependent on the chosen. As for the second, because the relation of the necessitator to all individuals is equal, so it would necessitate the existence of infinite power. And as for its division into command, prohibition, interrogation, information, and call, it is only according to relation. So that one speech, by virtue of its relation to something in a specific manner, is information, and by virtue of its relation to something else or in another manner, is command, and likewise for the rest. And it was said: His speech is five, the mentioned divisions. And Ibn Saʿīd of the Ashʿarīs said: It is in pre-eternity one and not qualified by any of those five, but it becomes one of them in the future. And it was objected against him: They are its types, so it does not exist without them, because a genus does not exist except within one of its types. And the answer: Denial of that for types that arise according to relation, meaning that they are not real types of it such that what you mentioned follows; rather, they are relational types that arise for it according to its relation to things, so it is permissible that its genus exist without them and with them. So the statement of Ibn Saʿīd is not very remote.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion (tanbīh) defending the unity of divine speech (kalām) and explaining how it can be described as command, prohibition, etc. without multiplicity. The author first presents an objection: if speech is one and differences are due to relations, why can't all attributes (knowledge, power, etc.) be reduced to one meaning, or even to the essence itself? The companions (aṣḥāb) respond that power and will have distinct effects (bringing into existence vs. specification), so the affecting agent must differ, whereas speech's relations produce no effect. The author notes this response has problems: the principle that effects cannot come from one agent is philosophical, not theological. Even if accepted, power would then need to vary with its effects, but the Ashʿarī view that existence is the essence itself leads to contradiction. Also, the response fails for non-effective attributes like knowledge and life. Then al-Āmidī's view is cited that the objection against unity of speech is difficult. The text then repeats the header and gives the standard Ashʿarī position: divine speech is one, proven by the argument from power (if multiple, they would either be chosen or necessitated, both impossible). Its division into five types (command, prohibition, interrogation, information, call) is only by relation (bi-ḥasab al-taʿalluq). One speech becomes information by relating to something in a certain manner, command by relating differently, etc. Then a different opinion (qīla) says speech is five distinct types. Ibn Saʿīd says it is one in pre-eternity and only becomes one of those in the future. The objection is that a genus cannot exist without its types. The answer denies that these are real types; they are relational (iʿtibāriyya), so the genus can exist without them. Thus Ibn Saʿīd's view is not far-fetched.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the five types of speech are real and distinct in God's essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies they are merely relational (iʿtibāriyya), preserving the oneness of the attribute.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari position explain that God's speech appears as command, prohibition, etc.?
A) By saying God has multiple speech attributes.
B) By saying the one speech relates to things in different ways.
C) By saying these are created sounds.
D) By saying God's speech changes over time.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is one; its diversity in revelation is due to the relation (taʿalluq) to different objects, not to multiplicity in the attribute itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's commands and prohibitions as coming from the same single eternal source, or do you pick and choose based on your convenience? How can this unity of divine speech change your attitude toward every word He has revealed?
?
The passage says that divine speech becomes command or prohibition only by relation to your actions. Are you aware that every command you encounter in revelation is a direct relation of that one speech to you? How does this make you accountable for every directive you ignore?
?
If you believe that God's speech is one, do you approach all parts of revelation—commands, prohibitions, news—with equal reverence? Or do you undervalue some because they seem less urgent? What step will you take this week to honor a command you have been neglecting?

Unity of God's Speech
Sanusi proves that God's speech is one using the same logic as for power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari scholars affirm that God's speech is one, based on the argument from divine power.

Core Idea
God's speech is one because multiple speeches would imply impossibility.

This lesson opens the discussion of God's speech by establishing its unity. Just as with God's power, His speech cannot be multiple without leading to absurdities. The passage draws on the earlier proof about power to show that speech, as an attribute, must be one.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice how it begins with an assertion of unity and then provides a proof.
The proof mirrors the earlier discussion on power.
2
Identify the two options rejected
The proof considers two ways multiple speeches could attach to the essence: by choice or by necessity. Both are rejected. Find these two options in the text.
3
Understand why each is invalid
For choice: the eternal cannot depend on a created choice. For necessity: it would lead to infinite power. Grasp the logical refutation of each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیه کلامه تعالى واحد عندنا لما مر في القدرة من أنها لو تعددت لاستندت إلى الذات إما بالاختيار أو بالإيجاب وهما باطلان أما الأول فلأن القديم لا يستند إلى المختار، وأما الثاني فلأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع الأعداد سواء فيلزم وجود قدر لا يتناهى وأما انقسامه إلى الأمر والنهي والاستفهام والخبر والنداء فإنما هو بحسب التعلق فذلك الكلام الواحد باعتبار تعلقه بشيء على وجه مخصوص يكون خبرا وباعتبار تعلقه بشيء آخر أو على وجه آخر يكون أمرا، وكذا الحال في البواقي وقيل كلامه خمسة هي الأقسام المذكورة، وقال ابن سعيد من الأشاعرة هو في الأزل واحد وليس متصفا بشيء من تلك الخمسة وإنما يصير أحدهما فيما لا يزال وأورد عليه أنها أنواعه فلا يوجد دونها إذ الجنس لا يوجد إلا في ضمن شيء من أنواعه والجواب منع ذلك في أنواع تحصل بحسب التعلق يعني أنها ليست أنواعا حقيقية له حتى يلزم ما ذكرتم، بل هي أنواع اعتبارية تحصل له بحسب تعلقه بالأشياء فجاز أن يوجد جنسها بدونها ومعها أيضا، فليس كلام ابن سعيد ببعيد جدا
Warning: His speech, may He be exalted, is one according to us for what has been mentioned in [the topic of] power, that if it were multiple, it would be attributed to the essence either by choice or by necessity, and both are invalid. As for the first, because the eternal does not depend on the chosen; as for the second, because the relation of the necessitator to all things is equal, so the existence of an infinite power would follow. As for its division into command, prohibition, interrogation, information, and call, it is only according to relation. That single speech, by virtue of its relation to something in a particular manner, is information, and by virtue of its relation to another thing or in another manner, is command, and similarly for the rest. And it was said that His speech is five, which are the mentioned divisions. And Ibn Sa'id of the Ash'aris said that it is in eternity one and not qualified by any of these five, but becomes one of them in what is yet to come. And it was objected against him that they are its species, so it does not exist without them, since the genus does not exist except within something of its species. And the answer: [we] deny that regarding species that arise according to relation, meaning that they are not real species for it such that what you mentioned follows; rather, they are accidental species that arise for it according to its relation to things, so it is possible that its genus exists without them and with them as well. Therefore, Ibn Sa'id's statement is not very far-fetched.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the attribute of God's speech. It begins by establishing that God's speech is one, based on the earlier discussion of power: if speech were multiple, it would be attributed to the essence either by choice or by necessity, both of which are invalid. The author then clarifies that the division of this one speech into command, prohibition, interrogation, information, and call is only by relation (tacalluq). The same single speech, depending on what it relates to and how, becomes an instance of one of these types. The author then presents the view of Ibn Sa'id (a prominent Ash'ari) that in eternity the speech is not qualified by any of these five divisions, but becomes one of them in time. The objection is raised that if these are species of speech, the genus cannot exist without them. The answer is that these are not real species but accidental (اعتبارية), meaning they are not inherent divisions of the eternal speech itself but arise from its relation to objects. Thus the genus (eternal speech) can exist without them. The author concludes that Ibn Sa'id's view is not far-fetched.

Key Terms
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't multiple divine speeches be attributed to the essence by choice?
A) Because choice implies imperfection.
B) Because the eternal does not depend on the chosen.
C) Because choice would make speech created.
D) Because choice would lead to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
God's speech must be one because multiple speeches would require an impossible dependence on choice or necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is one, how does that change the way you approach the Quran? Are you treating it as a single, coherent message from one Speaker, or do you pick and choose verses that fit your desires? What would it mean to submit to the whole of His speech?
?
The proof says that multiple speeches would require an impossible dependence. In your life, do you treat God's commands as a unified whole, or do you try to 'split' His speech to avoid some parts? Which command are you most reluctant to accept as part of His one speech? Make a firm intention today to obey it.
?
Reflect on the idea that God's speech is not multiple but one. When you hear different Qur'anic verses, do you sense a single divine Will behind them? If you struggle with apparent contradictions, commit to studying the Quran with the attitude that every verse comes from the same one speech, and therefore must be harmonized.

Division of Speech by Relation
Sanusi explains the five types of speech and defends the view that they are merely relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the single divine speech is divided into five types only by relation (tacalluq) and the debate over whether they are real species.

Core Idea
The five speech-types are relational, not real divisions of God's eternal speech.

Having established that God's speech is one, the text explains how it can still be called command, prohibition, etc. The answer is that these are not separate speeches but the same speech related to different objects and contexts. The passage also considers an objection from Ibn Sa'id, who argued that the speech is not qualified by these types in eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the entire passage from 'وأما انقسامه' to the end. Note the five types and the phrase 'بحسب التعلق'.
2
Understand the relational principle
The passage states that the same single speech becomes a command, prohibition, etc., based on its relation to different objects and manners. This is key.
3
Analyze Ibn Sa'id's view and the objection
Ibn Sa'id says the speech in eternity is not any of the five; the objection says that if they are species, the genus cannot exist without them. Identify both.
4
Grasp the answer and conclusion
The answer denies they are real species; they are accidental (اعتبارية). Thus the genus can exist without them. The author finds this plausible.

Checkpoint
How does the passage answer the objection that if the five types are species of speech, the genus cannot exist without them?
A) By conceding that the speech must have at least one type.
B) By denying that they are real species; they are accidental species.
C) By arguing that the speech can have multiple types simultaneously.
D) By asserting that the speech is five in eternity.

Key Takeaway
The five types of divine speech are relational, not real divisions, so the eternal speech is one and can exist without being any of these types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ibn Sa'id's view suggests that Allah's command to you exists only when it relates to you personally. Does this realization make you feel a greater urgency to respond to His commands, knowing they are directly addressing you in this moment?
?
The passage argues that the five types are not real divisions but relational. Have you ever treated certain parts of the Qur'an as less 'direct' because they are in a particular form (e.g., narrative vs. command)? How should this oneness of divine speech unify your engagement with the entire Qur'an?

The Eternity of the Relation of God's Speech: Ash'ari vs. Ibn Sa'id
First part of the chunk: the dispute over eternal/temporal relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core disagreement between al-Ash'ari and Ibn Sa'id regarding whether the relation (ta'alluq) of God's eternal speech is eternal or temporal.

Core Idea
The relation of God's speech is eternal for al-Ash'ari but temporal for Ibn Sa'id.

This passage introduces a foundational debate within Ash'ari theology. While both schools agree that God's speech divides into five types, they differ on whether the relation that produces these divisions is eternal or temporal. Al-Ash'ari affirms eternity, while Ibn Sa'id and many early scholars argue for temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the disagreement
Read from the beginning to the quote from al-Amidi. Notice the initial statement about the difficulty of the issue.
The word 'مشكل' indicates that this is a known problematic topic.
2
Identify the two positions
Al-Ash'ari says the relation is eternal; Ibn Sa'id says it is temporal. This is the core difference stated explicitly.
3
Note the agreement on fivefold division
Both schools agree that the division into five types is based on relation, despite their difference on the nature of that relation.
4
Consider al-Amidi's historical report
Al-Amidi confirms the same disagreement among early scholars, showing its significance.

والمتعلقات مشكل وعسى أن يكون عند غيري حله ولعسر جوابه ذهب بعض أصحابنا إلى القول بأن كلام الله تعالى القائم بذاته خمس صفات مختلفة . قوله: (فإنما هو بحسب التعلق وهذا التعلق أزلي عند الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري وبعض الأشاعرة وحادث عند أبي سعيد وطائفة كثيرة من المتقدمين وهذا هو الفرق بين المذهبين وإن توافقا في أن الانقسام إلى الأقسام الخمسة بحسب التعلق، قال الآمدي : في أبكار الأفكار : اختلفوا في وصف كلام الله تعالى في الأزل بكونه أمرا ونهيا وغيره من أقسام الكلام فأثبته الشيخ الأشعري ونفاه ابن سعيد وطائفة كثيرة من القدماء مع اتفاقهم على وصفه بذلك فيما لا يزال فإن قلت التعلقات المتعددة الأزلية ليست بالاختيار بل بالإيجاب فتوجه الإشكال على انحصار الاقسام في الخمسة لوجوب تعلقه حينئذ بكل ما يمكن أن يتعلق به، قلت : لعل الشيخ يمنع إمكان ما عدا هذا القدر من التعلق في كلامه تعالى وهذا بحسب النوع، وأما بحسب شخص كل نوع من الأنواع الخمسة فيجوز أن يكون التعلق حادثا بالاختيار وبهذا يندفع لزوم وجوب التكليف وإشكال حديث النسخ لكن المقام يعد محل تأمل فليتأمل . قوله : (فذلك الكلام الواحد قيل : كون كلام الله تعالى على هذه الصفة غير معقول فإن قوله : اقيموا الصلاة، مع قوله : ولا تقربوا الزنا كيف يتحدان في الأزل لفظا أو معنى حتى يتكثر بالاعتبارات، والحق أن الأمر مشكل إذا كان الكلام النفسي عين المدلول الوضعي للكلام اللفظي، وأما إذا كان التعبير باللفظي عن النفسي من قبيل التعبير بالأثر عن المؤثر كما مر فلا إشكال، فتأمل
And the relations are problematic, and perhaps their solution is with someone else. And because of the difficulty of its answer, some of our colleagues went to the statement that the speech of God Almighty, subsisting in His essence, is five different attributes. His statement: 'It is only according to relation, and this relation is eternal according to Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and some Ash'aris, and temporal according to Abu Sa'id and a large group of the early scholars, and this is the difference between the two schools, even though they agree that the division into five sections is according to relation.' Al-Amidi said in 'Abkar al-Afkar': They disagreed about describing the speech of God Almighty in eternity as being command, prohibition, etc. from the divisions of speech. Sheikh al-Ash'ari affirmed it, and Ibn Sa'id and a large group of the early scholars negated it, while they agreed on describing it as such in the future. If you say: The multiple eternal relations are not by choice but by necessity, so the problem arises regarding the restriction to five sections, because then His relation would be necessary to everything possible to relate to. I say: Perhaps the Sheikh denies the possibility of relation beyond this amount in His speech, and this is by type. As for by individual of each of the five types, it is possible that the relation is temporal by choice, and by this the necessity of obligation and the problem of abrogation are repelled. But the situation is worthy of reflection, so reflect. His statement: 'That single speech' – it was said: The state of God's speech in this manner is inconceivable, for His statement 'Establish prayer' along with His statement 'Do not approach zina', how can they be one in eternity verbally or in meaning so that they are multiplied by considerations? The truth is that the matter is problematic if the inner speech (kalam nafsi) is the very conventional meaning of verbal speech. But if the expression of verbal speech about inner speech is like expressing the effect about the cause, as was mentioned, then there is no problem. So reflect.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a key disagreement among Ash'ari theologians. It first notes that the nature of the relations (tacalluqat) is problematic, and some scholars suggested that God's eternal speech consists of five distinct attributes. The commentator then explains that the relation itself is considered eternal by al-Ash'ari and some followers, but temporal by Abu Sa'id (Ibn Sa'id) and many early scholars. This is the real difference between the two positions, even though both agree that the division into five is by relation. Al-Amidi is quoted to show the historical disagreement. An objection is raised: if the relations are eternal and necessary, then the speech would have to relate to all possible objects, not just five types. The answer is that al-Ash'ari limits the possibility of relation to these five types only. The second part addresses a different objection: how can one eternal speech be both a command and a prohibition? The answer distinguishes between inner speech (kalam nafsi) and verbal speech; if the inner speech is identical to the conventional meaning, it is problematic, but if the verbal expression is merely an effect of the inner speech, then no problem arises.

Key Terms
تعلق
أزلي
حادث
كلام نفسي
كلام لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement is about the speech itself being eternal or created.
Correction: The disagreement is specifically about the relation (ta'alluq) of the speech, not the speech itself. Both agree the speech itself is eternal (kalam nafsi).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main point of difference between al-Ash'ari and Ibn Sa'id?
A) The eternity of God's speech itself
B) The eternity of the relation of God's speech
C) The number of types of speech
D) Whether speech is an attribute at all

Key Takeaway
The core disagreement in this debate is whether the relation that gives rise to the five types of God's speech is eternal (al-Ash'ari) or temporal (Ibn Sa'id).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's command to you is an eternal decree, how would that change your response when you struggle to obey? Would you treat your disobedience as a momentary lapse or a rebellion against an everlasting order?
?
The scholars debated whether the relation of God's speech is eternal or temporal. How does your own intellectual struggle with theological details affect your relationship with God? Does it drive you to deeper reverence, or does it become a distraction from living out His commands?
?
Consider that the five divisions of God's speech (command, prohibition, etc.) exist because of a relation. In your daily life, which of these relations do you most need to be conscious of? Commit to one specific way you will respond to God's command or prohibition this week.

Objections to Eternal Relations and the Nature of Inner Speech
Second part: objections and their answers, including the role of inner speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against the doctrine of eternal relations and how the distinction between inner speech (kalam nafsi) and verbal speech resolves the problem of multiple opposite statements.

Core Idea
If relations are eternal and necessary, God's speech would relate to all possibilities, but al-Ash'ari limits it to five types; further, opposite commands can coexist in inner speech.

This passage addresses two objections to the Ash'ari view on eternal relations. First, if the relation is eternal and necessary, why is it limited to five types? Second, how can one eternal speech contain both commands and prohibitions? The answers involve restricting the possibility of relation and distinguishing between inner and verbal speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
Read the objection starting with 'فإن قلت'. It argues that eternal necessary relations would force God's speech to relate to all possible objects.
The term 'بالإيجاب' indicates necessity.
2
Understand the answer to the first objection
Al-Ash'ari denies the possibility of relation beyond these five types; the relation is restricted by type.
This allows for temporal relation by choice within each type.
3
Consider the second objection
How can a single eternal speech be both a command and a prohibition?
4
Examine the solution: inner vs. verbal speech
If verbal speech expresses inner speech as an effect expresses a cause, there is no problem.
The distinction is crucial to resolve the contradiction.

The Eternal Speech Is an Accidental Genus
Clarifies that eternal speech is an accidental genus relative to its accidental species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal speech of Allah is considered an accidental genus rather than a real genus, and how this resolves an objection about the existence of a genus without species.

Core Idea
The eternal speech is an accidental genus, not a real genus, so objections about missing species do not apply.

In this passage, the commentator defends Ibn Sa'id's view that the five types of divine speech are not real species under a real genus. Instead, the relationship is conceptual: the eternal speech is an accidental genus relative to these accidental species. This brief but crucial clarification resolves a logical objection that would otherwise undermine the doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms 'accidental genus' and 'accidental species'.
The passage is a continuous response to an imagined objection.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage makes an assertion: the five types are accidental species. Then it draws an analogy: the eternal speech is an accidental genus. Finally it concludes that the eternal speech is not a real genus, contrary to what objectors assumed.
The phrase 'فلا كما توهموه' directly refutes the objectors.
3
Understand the consequence
Because the genus is accidental, it does not require actual distinct species. This is why it is possible for the genus to exist without them—a key point against the objection.
This preserves the unity and simplicity of God's attribute of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فجاز أن يوجد جنسها بدونها كما أن الأنواع الخمسة انواع اعتبارية بالنسبة إلى مطلق كلامه تعالى، كذلك هو جنس اعتباري بالقياس إليها إذ الحق أن الكلام الأزلي ليس جنساً فلا كما توهموه
His statement: 'So it is possible that its genus exists without them, just as the five types are accidental species with respect to the absolute speech of God Almighty, so too it is an accidental genus in relation to them, for the truth is that the eternal speech is not a genus. Thus, it is not as they imagined.'
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a brief explanatory comment defending Ibn Sa'id's position. It states that the five types are accidental species (أنواع اعتبارية) relative to God's absolute speech, and correspondingly, the eternal speech itself is an accidental genus (جنس اعتباري) relative to those species. In other words, the eternal speech is not a real genus that must have species; rather, the genus-species relationship here is merely conceptual and relational. Therefore, the objection that a genus cannot exist without its species does not apply, because it is not a real genus. The commentator concludes with 'thus it is not as they imagined', meaning the critics' assumption that the eternal speech is a real genus is mistaken.

Key Terms
جنس اعتباري
نوع اعتباري
كلام أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five types are real species under a real genus of eternal speech.
Correction: The eternal speech is only an accidental genus, and the five types are accidental species, so no real genus-species requirement applies.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that the eternal speech is not a real genus?
A) Because it is impossible for speech to be a genus.
B) Because the five types are accidental species, and the eternal speech is an accidental genus in relation to them.
C) Because Ibn Sa'id denied that the eternal speech has any relation.
D) Because the genus would be multiple.

Key Takeaway
The eternal speech is not a real genus; it is an accidental genus, so the objection about missing species is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the fact that the eternal speech is a single undifferentiated attribute, not composed of separate 'species'. When you read the Qur'an, do you treat it as a unified speech from God, or do you mentally split it into categories? What does this teach you about the oneness of God's attribute of speech?
?
The objectors assumed a real genus-species relationship in God's speech. Are there any attributes of God that you have similarly compartmentalized or limited based on your own reasoning? How can you guard against imposing human concepts on the Divine?
?
The passage concludes 'thus it is not as they imagined.' How often do you find your own imagination limiting your understanding of God? What specific step can you take today to submit your intellect to the revealed truth about His attributes?

Mu'tazila Arguments and Ash'ari Refutation
Mu'tazila arguments and Ash'ari response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments used by the Mu'tazila to prove that lying is impossible for God and the Ash'ari response rejecting the obligation of the most beneficial.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue from good/evil and welfare; the Ash'aris deny any obligation upon God.

The passage begins by stating the consensus that lying is impossible for God. It then presents the two proofs of the Mu'tazila: first, that lying is an evil action, which God cannot do; second, that lying would undermine trust in divine revelation, contradicting God's obligation to do what is most beneficial for creation. The Ash'ari response is to deny that any action is obligatory upon God, including the most beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila arguments
Read the first argument: lying is an evil action. Note that the Mu'tazila base this on rational good and evil. Then read the second argument about welfare.
The text mentions 'you will know its invalidity' referring to the first argument.
2
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
The response denies that anything is obligatory upon God, including the most beneficial. This rejects the Mu'tazila premise.
No action is obligatory on God.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this difference shapes the two schools' approaches to divine attributes and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفريع على ثبوت الكلام لله تعالى وهو أنه يمتنع عليه الكذب اتفاقا أما عند المعتزلة فلوجهين : الأول أنه أي الكذب في الكلام الذي هو عندهم من قبيل الأفعال دون الصفات قبيح وهو سبحانه لا يفعل القبيح وهو بناء على أصلهم في إثبات حكم العقل بحسن الأفعال وقبحها مقيسة إلى الله تعالى وستعرف بطلانه . الثاني : أنه مناف لمصلحة العالم لأنه إذا جاز وقوع الكذب في كلامه ارتفع الوثوق عن إخباره بالثواب والعقاب وسائر ما أخبر به من أحوال الآخرة والأولى وفي ذلك فوات مصالح لا تحصى والأصلح واجب عليه تعالى عندهم يجوز إخلاله به والجواب : منع وجوب الأصلح، إذ لا يجب عليه شيء أصلا بل هو عن ذلك قطعا، وأما امتناع الكذب عليه عندنا فثلاثة أوجه : الأول. أنه نقص والنقص على الله تعالى محال إجماعا، وأيضا يلزم على تقدير أن يقع الكذب في كلامه أن تكون نحن أكمل منه في بعض الأوقات أعني وقت صدقنا في كلامنا، وهذا الوجه إنما يدل على أن الكلام النفسي الذي هو صفة قائمة بذاته يكون صادقا، وإلا لزم النقصان في صفته تعالى مع كمال صفتنا ولا يدل على صدقه في الحروف والكلمات التي يخلقها في جسم دالة على معان مقصودة، ولما كان لقائل أن يقول خلق الكاذب أيضا نقص في فعله فيعود المحذور بعينه أشار إلى متعال بل أمرا معينا يعرضه الإضافات وله أسماء بحسب كل إضافة نوعية كذا ذكره الأبهري . قوله: (والجواب منع وجوب الأصلح فإن قلت : سلمنا ذلك لكن الكذب في الكلام ينافي حكمة إرسال الرسل ويجاب بأن إحاطة كل حكمة مما لا يأتي في القوى القاصرة فلعل فيما فعله حكمة جليلة لا تطلع عليها . قوله : ( والنقص على الله تعالى محال إجماعا فإن قلت : لا شك أن عدم إرساله تعالى نوحا عليه السلام إلى قومه ممكن لأن الإرسال غير واجب عليه تعالى بل واقع باختياره، والإنكار مكابرة فيلزم إمكان النقص قلت : إمكان النقص إنما يلزم إذا أمكن اجتماع عدم الإرسال مع صدور قوله تعالى : إنا أرسلنا نوحا إلى قومه وهو ممنوع، وامتناع هذا الاجتماع لا ينافي إمكان عدم الإرسال في نفسه بناء على قاعدة الاختيار فتدبر. قوله : (أشار إلى دفعه بقوله : واعلم إلخ) لا يخفى أن ظاهر قول المصنف واعلم إلخ هو الاعتراض على الوجه الأول المذكور في المتن ولما توجه عليه أن اللازم من الكذب النقص من جهة صفته الذاتية لا النقص في الفعل، والمتنازع في جواز الثاني لا الأول فإنه ممتنع بالإجماع كما ستعرف تكلف الشارح في توجيه كلامه بتقدير سؤال بأن الدليل المذكور لا يدل على امتناع الكذب في كلامه اللفظي، وجواب بأنه يدل عليه أيضا لأن خلق الكاذب نقص في فعله وجعل هذا الكلام اعتراضا على الجواب المقدر، وأنت خبير بأن هذا لا يخلو عن تمحل، لكن
A branch [from the proof] of the establishment of speech for God Almighty, which is that lying is impossible for Him by consensus. As for the Mu'tazila, [they argue] with two reasons: first, that lying in speech—which according to them is of the category of actions, not attributes—is evil, and He, glorified, does not do evil, based on their principle of affirming rational judgment of good and evil in actions measured against God Almighty, and you will know its invalidity. Second, that it conflicts with the welfare of the world, because if lying in His speech were possible, trust would be lost in His reports of reward, punishment, and all that He informed of about the states of the Hereafter and this world, and in that there is loss of countless benefits, and [according to them] the most beneficial is obligatory upon Him, so it is not permissible for Him to neglect it. The answer: [we] deny the obligation of the most beneficial, for nothing is obligatory upon Him at all, rather He is absolutely free from that. As for the impossibility of lying upon Him according to us, [it is] through three aspects: First, that it is a deficiency, and deficiency upon God Almighty is impossible by consensus. Also, if lying occurred in His speech, it would follow that we are more perfect than Him at some times, namely when we are truthful in our speech. This aspect only indicates that the inner speech which is an attribute subsisting in His essence is truthful; otherwise, deficiency would follow in His attribute despite the perfection of our attribute, and it does not indicate the truthfulness of the letters and words that He creates in a body indicating intended meanings. And as someone might say: creating a liar is also a deficiency in action, so the same problem returns. He pointed to... nay, it is a specific thing that is subject to relations and has names according to each specific relation—so mentioned al-Abhari. His statement: 'And the answer: denial of the obligation of the most beneficial.' If you say: we grant that, but lying in speech contradicts the wisdom of sending messengers. The answer: that the comprehension of every wisdom is not within limited powers; perhaps there is a profound wisdom in what He does that you do not perceive. His statement: 'And deficiency upon God Almighty is impossible by consensus.' If you say: there is no doubt that His not sending Noah, peace be upon him, to his people is possible because sending is not obligatory upon Him, rather it occurs by His choice, and denial is obstinacy, so the possibility of deficiency follows. I say: the possibility of deficiency only follows if it were possible for the non-sending to coincide with His statement 'Indeed We sent Noah to his people', and that is forbidden. The impossibility of this coincidence does not contradict the possibility of non-sending in itself, based on the principle of choice, so ponder. His statement: 'He pointed to its refutation by saying: 'Know, etc.' It is not hidden that the apparent meaning of the author's statement 'Know, etc.' is an objection to the first aspect mentioned in the text. And since it was objected against him that the consequence of lying is deficiency in terms of His essential attribute, not deficiency in action, and the point of contention is the permissibility of the second, not the first, for it is impossible by consensus as you will know. The commentator strained in explaining his statement by hypothesizing a question that the mentioned proof does not indicate the impossibility of lying in His verbal speech, and answering that it does indicate it as well because creating a liar is a deficiency in action, and he made this statement an objection to the hypothesized answer. And you are aware that this is not free of artificiality, but...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive proof of the impossibility of lying for God, including arguments from both Mu'tazila and Ash'aris, along with objections and answers. It begins by stating the consensus that lying is impossible for God. It then presents the two Mu'tazila arguments: first, lying is evil and God does not do evil (based on their theory of objective good and evil); second, lying would contradict divine wisdom (salvation of humanity) and God must do what is most beneficial. The Ash'ari answer to the Mu'tazila is to deny the obligation of the most beneficial—nothing is obligatory upon God. Then the Ash'ari positive proof is given in three aspects. The first aspect is that lying would be a deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for God. Moreover, if God lied, then a truthful human would be more perfect than God in that respect. However, this argument only applies to inner speech (kalam nafsi), not to the verbal speech created in bodies. The second and third aspects are not fully elaborated in this text, but the discussion continues with objections. An objection is raised that even creating false verbal speech would be a deficiency in action. The text then quotes al-Abhari's response. Later, an objection is considered: if God did not send a prophet, that would be a deficiency. The answer is that the deficiency only follows if God's statement of sending contradicts the actual non-sending; but since God's speech is one, if He said He sent Noah, then not sending would be impossible. The passage ends with a critical note on the author's (Iji's) wording and the commentator's (Jurjani's) interpretation.

Key Terms
كذب
قبيح
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is obligated to do what is most beneficial for creation (as the Mu'tazila claim).
Correction: The Ash'ari response denies that anything is obligatory upon God, including the most beneficial.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila's second argument about welfare?
A) They accept the obligation of al-aslah but argue it is fulfilled.
B) They deny that anything is obligatory upon God.
C) They argue lying is not actually harmful.
D) They claim God's actions are beyond moral judgment.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila rely on rational good/evil and divine obligation; the Ash'aris deny any obligation on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that if lying were possible, trust in God's promises and warnings would be lost. Honestly, do you fully trust every word Allah has spoken—even the parts that challenge your desires? If you truly believed He never lies, would you obey Him differently today? What is one promise you can act on right now?
?
The Ash'ari response says nothing is obligatory on God. Does this thought make you uncomfortable? Do you sometimes feel God 'owes' you something—health, success, or happiness? Examine your heart. How can you let go of expectations and embrace a posture of pure gratitude and servitude?
?
The Mu'tazila rely on their own reasoning to judge good and evil. How much do you rely on your own sense of fairness when reading Allah's commands? Are there areas where you secretly think, 'That seems unfair'? Confront that thought with the truth that Allah has no obligations—yet He chooses mercy. What does that demand from you?
